Preface 
This thesis has been submitted on February 23rd 2015 as a partial fulfillment for the requirements to obtain 
the degree of Master of Science in Chemical and Biochemical Engineering at the Technical University of 
Denmark. The thesis is equal to 32.5 ECTS-points and the work has been done at the Computer-Aided 
Process Engineering Center (CAPEC) at the department of Chemical and Biochemical Engineering, under the 
supervision of Professor Rafiqul Gani.

==PAGE:4==
 
 
 iii | P a g e  
 
Abstract 
In this work, a computer-aided framework for assessment of the overall sustainability potential of chemical 
process flowsheets has been applied to 3 different base case process flowsheets. For each case, the main 
objective was to study the flowsheet with respect to their sustainability potential, in order to suggest more 
sustainable design alternatives without trade-offs with respect to the sustainability metrics.  
The first process flowsheet involves a production of cumene from propylene and benzene. In this case, the 
utilization of the computer-aided framework led to the proposal of 4 more sustainable design alternatives. 
Out of these, only one of the proposed alternatives turned out to be a non-tradeoff solution, offering a 
reduction in the raw material loss of 99.62%, as well as a reduction in the overall utility costs by 38.36%, 
aspects that both ended up rendering the process economically feasible. In addition to this the alternative 
design was also associated with an energy demand reduction of 14.38 %, partially also leading to 38.75% 
reduction in the overall carbon footprint.   
The second process flowsheet regards a HDA production of benzene from toluene and hydrogen. For this 
case study, 3 more sustainable design alternatives were proposed, all being non-tradeoff solutions. The one 
that best met the target specifications offered a reduction in the overall utility cost of 66.26% and a 651% 
improvement of the net profit. Furthermore an energy demand reduction of 44.29% and an overall carbon 
footprint reduction of 45.37% had been achieved.  
The last process flowsheet involves a bioethanol production process from lignocellulosic biomass. Here 4 
design alternatives were established. The one found to be the best solution offered a 61.2% raw material 
reduction as well as a 32.1% decline in the overall utility cost, accompanied by an 823% improvement of 
the net profit. With respect to the overall energy requirement, this was only reduced by 3.13%, but a large 
quantity of heating duty was eliminated while only cooling duties emerged, explaining why a great 
considerable utility cost reduction was achieved. However the altered steam preparation method mainly 
responsible for the increased profit, required a compromise with respect to the carbon footprint, which 
was increased by 41.13%.  
Through the treatment of these three base cases, it was simultaneously shown, that the computer-aided 
framework is generic and can be considered a highly valuable tool in assessing the sustainability potential 
of chemical processes. 
 
 
 
==PAGE:5==
 
 
 iv | P a g e  
 
Dansk resumé 
I dette projekt, bliver en metode understøttet af forskellige computer værktøjer, der specielt er blevet 
etableret til at hjælpe med at analyse af kemiske processer, med henblik på deres bæredygtige potentiale, 
anvendt på tre forskellige kemiske processer. I alle tilfælde var formålet at analysere disse processer med 
hensyn til deres bæredygtige potentiale, med henblik på at generere mere bæredygtige design alternativer 
der ikke kræver kompromiser med hensyn til de bæredygtige aspekter.  
Den første proces omhandlede en produktion af cumene baseret på propylene og benzene. Here blev der 
ved hjælp af den anvendte metode fundet frem til 4 mere bæredygtige design alternativer. Den af disse der 
blev fundet til at være den bedste løsning tilbød en nedskæring på råmateriale tabet på 99.62% og en 
reducering af utility omkostningerne på 38.36%. Disse to aspekter tillod at processen blev rentabel. 
Yderligere tilbød denne design alternativ en reduktion af energi behovet på 14.38% samt en samlet carbon 
footprint reduktion på 38.75%.  
Den anden kemiske process der blev behandlet, omhandlede produktionen af benzene fra toluene og 
hydrogen via hydrodealkylation. Her blev der etableret 3 mere bæredygtige design alternativer. Det 
alternative som blev fundet til at være den bedste løsning tilbød en nedskæring af utility omkostningerne 
på 66.26%, samt en 651% forbedring af nettoindkomsten. Derudover opnår den også en reduktion af energi 
behovet på 44.29% samt en samlet carbon footprint reduktion på 45.37%. 
For den sidste kemiske proces, der omhandlede produktionen af bioethanol fra et lignocellulose-baseret 
råmateriale, blev der genereret 4 design alternativer. Den af disse fire der blev betragtet som det bedste 
alternativ var associeret med nedskæringer i råmateriale tab på i alt 61.2%, samt en 32.1% reduktion af den 
totale utility omkostning som resulterede i en forbedring på 823% med hensyn til rentabiliteten. Energy 
behovet blev dog kun reduceret med 3.13%, men dette indebar en reduktion af behovet for varmetilførsel, 
delvis erstattet af behovet for køling, som forklare hvorfor der alligevel opsnås forbedringer med hensyn til 
utility omkostninger. Desværre indebar alternativet en ændring af damp produktionen, som hovedsagelig 
er ansvarlig for den økonomiske forbedring, men som krævede et kompromi med hensyn til carbon 
footprint, der blev forhøjet med 41.13% 
Via behandlingen af de tre processer, blev det imidlertidigt også vist, at metoden kan bruges på alle 
kemiske processer, af varierende natur, og samtidig at det også er et værdifuldt værktøj, når 
bæredygtigheds potentialet af en kemisk proces skal vurderes.

1 | P a g e  
 
1 Introduction 
The concept of sustainable design is often used about a technology’s ability to ensure consumption of a 
natural resource without depleting or permanently damaging it. This often implies the use of resources that 
are renewable or alternatively the utilization of abundant alternatives in place of disappearing scarce 
natural resources.  
It is often associated with environmental aspects in conjunction with the increasing demand from existing 
technologies on limited natural resources. However in this work, which deals with sustainable design of 
chemical process flowsheets, the term sustainable design has a broader meaning. The general aspect of 
resource-consumption and environmental impact is imbedded, but a number of other aspects are also 
taken into account. 
Sustainable chemical process design is thought of as one, that not only entails a qualified treatment of 
material resources, which minimizes the consumption and loss of these, but also incorporates optimal 
solutions with respect to minimization of: Energy demands, damaging gas emissions and the resultant 
environmental impacts and expenses as well. Therefore in this work, sustainable design should be 
understood as an attempt to establish processes with high performances with regard to all the above 
mentioned sustainability metrics, and not only the general aspect associated with sustainability.    
As already mentioned, this works deals with the subject of sustainable process flowsheet design of 
chemical process. The main objective is to study the design of existing chemical process flowsheets, with 
respect to their sustainability potential, in order to suggest more sustainable design alternatives without 
trade-offs with respect to the sustainability metrics. A number of strategies are possible in order to 
complete the described task. In this work, a multilevel computer-aided framework for systematic design of 
more sustainable chemical processes is utilized. The framework incorporates the use of fitting computer 
tools alongside with an ordered workflow methodology that allows process criteria analysis by 
comprehensive assessment of environmental, economic and technical constraints.  
The comprehensive analysis leads to the identification of critical points (bottlenecks) within the process 
that hold high potential for improvement. By evaluating these, design target specifications are established 
in a manner, that if met, the bottlenecks are partially or in some cases completely overcome. Based on the 
target specifications, alternative retrofit designs are then attempted proposed. The generated alternatives 
are then subsequently screened in terms of ability to address the identified bottlenecks and match the set 
of improved target specifications, in order to determine the one that best overcomes the identified 
bottlenecks.  
==PAGE:10==
2 | P a g e  
 
The framework therefore functions as a decision-making tool, allowing the user to first generate better 
competing and enhanced process design solutions, in terms of environmental feasibility, sustainability and 
economic profitability and subsequently aiding in the determination of the overall best alternative design 
solution.   
Initially the computer-aided framework is applied to a conceptual example regarding a cumene production 
process in order to illustrate the methodology and workflow of the framework. Subsequently, the method 
is implemented to two case studies regarding the production of benzene from toluene and the production 
of bioethanol from lignocellulosic biomass. 
For all three processes assessed in this work, the main focus lies on identifying areas within the process 
that are responsible for high raw material loss and that are associated with high energy demands, both 
aspects that increase the expenses of a process, which can be improved by means of mass and heat 
integration. In this manner, three different types of processes  are analyzed comprehensively in an attempt 
to propose more sustainable and competitive design solutions without any trade-offs, while at the same 
time verifying the performance of the computer-aided framework.  
It is assumed that the reader has a chemical engineering background and sufficient knowledge regarding 
terms and parameters used within this field.  
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:11==
3 | P a g e  
 
2 Methodology 
First the concept and procedure of the computer-aided framework is outlined as given by Gargalo et al. (1) 
and Morales et al. (2). Subsequently a short description of the computer tools utilized in the framework is 
given, describing their required inputs and important outputs. For a more detailed description, reference is 
made to the manuals of these softwares. Following this, some additional required theory is stated. 
2.1 Computer-aided framework 
The overall purpose is to take a given process and attempt to come up with a more sustainable version, 
which achieves the same product specifications as the base case design. Therefore the starting point is that 
a process flowsheet converting raw materials into products exists. This could be a conceptual process, a 
published process, an existing industrial process or may even be a process which seemingly already has 
been optimized. Whichever of the above, the process flowsheet is taken as the base case design to be 
analyzed in order to investigate whether a more sustainable solution can be proposed.  
This task requires assessment of the process performance with respect to the main objectives embedded in 
the idea of sustainable design; resource and energy consumption, economic feasibility and environmental 
impact. Such a task requires a systematic approach, and in the light of the increased need for optimized, 
sustainable processes, a multi-criteria evaluation tool consisting of a multilevel computer-aided framework 
has been proposed for this purpose. As the name suggests, integrated in the framework, is the use of 
multiple computer tools which helps ease the task and maintain the overview. 
The framework consists of a series of steps including 3 analyzes that have been incorporated in order to 
study the main aspects of process sustainability. Together the three analyzes constitute a full sustainability 
analysis and the outcome is meant to raise awareness towards the areas within the process that constitute 
hotspots. The first analysis, a mass and energy indicators analysis, evaluates the resource and energy 
usages in order to identify paths within the process associated with valued material loss and/or high energy 
demand costs. The second analysis, a full economic analysis, addresses the aspect of the process economy 
and profitability and identifies unit operations responsible for high operational costs. The third analysis, an 
environmental impact analysis, addresses the aspect of greenhouse gas emissions and identifies the units 
associated with the highest carbon footprints.  
The idea is that the mass and energy indicators analysis emphasizes the paths of the process that are 
associated with high energy costs and valuable raw material loss with high potential for improvement. The 
paths associated with valued material loss are dealt with in a manner that reduces the loss. This may be in 
terms of mass integration by recycling. Another way is by increasing the separation efficiency of the 
==PAGE:12==
4 | P a g e  
 
separating unit operations in the downstream process. For the case of the paths with high energy costs, the 
units included in those paths may be considered as potential bottlenecks on the energy cost front. However 
in order to single out the units in the given paths, responsible for the majority of the energy cost, a full 
economic analysis is performed, which includes identification of the units with the highest utility costs. 
These units most often are also those that are connected with the highest energy consumptions and 
consequently will also be those associated the highest carbon footprints. Therefore by performing the last 
analysis, these units should once again get recognized, alongside with those unit operations with high 
energy demands, but only associated with a low operating cost. These are often unit operations utilizing 
cooling water, which has a low cost.  
The unit operations identified by all analyses are categorized as global bottlenecks. Lowering the energy 
consumption of these units or alternatively retrofitting them with a better solution that yields lower energy 
duties, a lower overall utility cost as well as carbon footprint can be obtained, assuming of course that the 
other unit’s duties aren’t affected negatively by the alteration. In this way these three analyses detect the 
process hotspots, which are to be targeted for improvement if a more sustainable solution is to be found. 
Improved design specifications are set for these targets, and measures can then be taken to generate new 
design alternatives which meet the target specifications. If a new design alternative overcomes some or all 
the target specifications, it follows that the alternative design is more sustainable than the base case 
design. If the improvements are achieved in such a manner that does not negatively affect other 
sustainability metrics, a non-tradeoff solution has been developed.  
Finally, the new design alternatives are screened in the same manner by conducting all three analyzes 
again, for each new design alternative. The results then form a basis for comparison of all the alternatives 
towards the base case design. In this way the framework enables the designer to link between 
sustainability potential, process debottlenecking, improvements and possible tradeoffs.  Regardless of the 
goal being to examine the performance and sustainability of a conceptual design, or to optimize and 
retrofit an already existing production process, the framework is meant to serve as a decision-making tool 
towards identifying a process alternative that best overcomes the process bottlenecks. 
The optimal outcome is a new design alternative with no tradeoffs that meets all the set design target 
specifications, and this is of course the main objective every time.  
The framework can be divided into three main parts: 
 
Part 1: Process simulation, full sustainability analysis and bottleneck identification 
 
Part 2: Generation and screening of new design alternatives 
==PAGE:13==
5 | P a g e  
 
 
Part 3: Comparison of base case design against new design alternatives 
Each part comprises a series of steps to be taken, as given below: 
 
Part 1. 
o Step 1.1: Base case rigorous simulation 
o Step 1.2: Mass and energy indicators analysis 
o Step 1.3: Full economic evaluation 
o Step 1.4: Environmental impact analysis 
o Step 1.5: Design targets 
 
Part 2. 
o Step 2.1: Generation of new design alternatives 
o Step 2.2: New design alternatives rigorous simulation 
o Step 2.3: Full sustainability analysis of new design alternatives 
 
Part 3. 
o Step 3.1: Comparison of full sustainability results 
o Step 3.2: Selection of best alternative 
An overview and illustration of the workflow is seen in Figure 2-1. The steps to be taken are given by the 
square boxes, while the computer tool utilized in that step are given in the circular boxes.  
In the following subsections the steps are described in more detail.

2.1.1 Step 1.1: Base case rigorous simulation 
In this step the rigorous simulation of the base case process flowsheet is to be performed using a process 
simulator. The main objective of the simulation is to generate process data for the analysis, mainly in the 
form of mass and energy balance data and equipment duties. In this work the process simulator used is 
PRO II version 9.3. A short description of this software is given later in section 2.2.  
Before the simulation can take place, all the necessary process information must be gathered. The 
simulation will require raw material rates, feed stream compositions, operational conditions, reaction 
parameters and technical data. For example the number of trays, feed tray, separation efficiency, or 
alternatively the reflux ratio, is needed for modeling distillation columns. Reaction stoichiometrics and 
conversions are necessary with respect to reactors, and split fractions for splitters.  
Once all the necessary data is available, the process is established and simulated in PRO II, which performs 
mass and energy balances and calculates equipment duties. If given the necessary specifications, PRO II also 
can estimate some equipment sizing parameters.  
The required input data and output data that results from this step are summarized below in Table 2-1 (1). 
Table 2-1. Tools required, data inputs and outputs associated with step 1.1.

2.1.2 Step 1.2: Mass and energy indicators analysis 
In this study, the primary targets are to achieve reductions in energy demand costs and valuable material 
waste. The mass and energy indicators analysis will emphasize the paths within the process that are 
associated with the highest material loss and energy consumption costs. The analysis is conducted by the 
means of the computer tool SustainPro which incorporates the method of Carvalho et al. (2008) (3). A short 
description of this software is given later in section 2.2.  
The analysis will require specified general data concerning the process, as well as detailed mass and energy 
balance data from the rigorous simulation. Additional data regarding pure component temperature 
dependent properties (thermal capacity, density and enthalpy of vaporization) are also required. The latter 
==PAGE:16==
8 | P a g e  
 
can be estimated by means of the software ICAS, which also is explained further in section 2.2. Another 
alternative is to use the pure component property correlations found in PRO II’s databank.  
Based on the input data, the process is broken down into open paths and closed cycle paths, with respect 
to mass and energy. For each the mass-based paths, various mass and energy indicators are determined 
and given in an ordered list, highlighting process bottlenecks regarding resource loss and energy costs, 
which hold potential for improvement. An improvement of these can be achieved by means of mass and 
heat integration. The opportunity for heat and mass integration arises when the mass indictors related to 
open paths have large negative values and the energy indicators have large positive values.  
The required input data and most important output data that results from this step are summarized below 
in Table 2-2 (1). 
Table 2-2. Tools required, data input and outputs associated with step 1.2. 
Step 
Data input 
Tools utilized 
Data output 
1.2 
 


reactions 




2.1.3 Step 1.3: Full economic evaluation 
The mass and energy indicator analysis identifies paths that have high energy consumptions costs, but does 
not point out the unit operations involved in these paths that are responsible for the majority of the costs. 
Therefore it is necessary to perform an additional analysis to single these out. The objective of this step is 
to perform a full economic analysis with the main purpose of identifying hotspots with respect to cost. 
These hotspots will be the equipment associated with the highest utility costs, and these will often also be 
those with the highest energy demands.  
The utility cost alone does not describe the overall profitability of the process. Therefore the capital and 
operating costs are also determined in order to perform a full economic evaluation using the excel-based 
tool ECON. A more detailed description of this tool follows in section 2.2.  
==PAGE:17==
9 | P a g e  
 
Based on sizing parameters, equipment purchase costs can be estimated by ECON. If duties and the 
required utilities are supplied, utility costs estimates are also determined. Once the purchase and utility 
costs are available, the capital costs and operating costs can be estimated. Taking the latter into account, 
ECON can conduct a final economic evaluation yielding process profitability metrics.  
The required input data and output data that results from this step are summarized below in Table 2-3 (1). 
Table 2-3. Tools required and data input and outputs associated with step 1.3. 
Step



 
Raw material and product prices 




2.1.4 Step 1.4: Environmental impact analysis 
The economical evaluation will identify the unit operations with the highest utility costs, and hence point 
towards the unit operations where the largest savings in terms of energy costs can be achieved. However 
unfortunately, the expenses and overall profitability of a process is not everything. Reduction of 
greenhouse gas emissions is a key aspect when speaking of environmental impact and has been a hot topic 
in recent years, due to the accelerating greenhouse gas emissions, negatively affecting the phenomenon 
known as global warming.  
An environmental impact analysis that determines the carbon footprint of a process is therefore valuable 
insight towards possible areas within a process, where emission reductions can be achieved. Therefore the 
objective of this step is to utilize the excel-based macro tool known as LCSoft. LCSoft incorporates the 
method of LCA (Life cycle assessment).  
In this study LCSoft is only used to determine the carbon footprint associated with each piece of process 
equipment. For this LCSoft requires mass balance data, equipment duties and specification of the type of 
utilities used. Units associated with the highest energy consumptions will often also be those associated 
with the highest carbon footprints, however the type of utility also plays an important role. A short 
description of LCSoft is given in section 2.2.4. 
The required input data and output data that results from this step are summarized in Table 2-4 (1). 
==PAGE:18==
10 | P a g e  
 
Table 2-4. Tools required and data input and outputs associated with step 1.4. 
Step 
Data input 
Tools utilized 
Data output



2.1.5 Step 1.5: Design Targets 
Once the three analyzes have been completed, the hot spots that hold potential for improvement should 
be apparent and the next step is to set improved target specifications, that the new design alternatives 
should achieve. The results from the mass and energy indicators analysis are cross-checked with the results 
obtained from the full economic analysis and the environmental impact analysis. If the critical points 
identified by SustainPro match the results obtained by ECON and LCSoft, global process bottleneck have 
been recognized and are obvious design targets. However, global bottlenecks are not always found. Once 
the design targets have been highlighted, target specifications to address the bottlenecks are set.  
2.1.6 Step 2.1: Generation of new design alternatives 
Taking the target specifications into account, new design alternatives are generated in order to overcome 
the identified bottlenecks. Several techniques can be used to solve this task, dependent on the type of the 
bottleneck.  
Heat integration is a way of reducing the heating and cooling duties by integrating process streams that are 
to be cooled, with process streams that are to be heated, in a single heat exchanger. If the temperatures of 
the two process streams allow an adequate temperature difference, two process streams can be brought to 
exchange heat, allowing the stream in need of cooling to transfer heat to the process stream requiring heat 
input. In this manner the utility requirements and associated costs can be reduced, or even in some cases 
completely eliminated. Therefore in situations where heat exchangers are associated with high utility costs, 
heat integration can be utilized to bring down these costs, if the conditions of the process streams allow it.  
When dealing with distillation columns, a reduction in energy demand can be obtained by simply 
redesigning the column. By increasing the number of theoretical steps and consequently the capital 
investment, reduced duties of the connected reboilers and condenser may be achieved. However this is 
only feasible if the energy savings achieved are large enough to pay back the increased capital within a 
short period of time.   
Often, another aspect that highly increases the operating expenses of a process, is the aspect of material 
loss. Valuable raw materials may be lost in larger quantities through waste streams or other output 
==PAGE:19==
11 | P a g e  
 
streams. In such situations mass integration in the form of establishment of recycle loops will reduce the 
loss and consequently the required input of the raw material, requiring that the raw material can be 
reused. Another solution could be to improve the recovery efficiencies of the purifying equipment. In the 
situation of solvent-based separations, selection of a more efficient solvent may reduce the solvent 
requirement, reducing the loads and hence duties of the unit operations involved.  
PRO II is not only a valuable tool for simulating and acquiring mass and energy balance data. It has proven 
to be just as appreciated for the task of generating alternative designs. It allows one to establish and test 
alternative ideas. In the case of stand-alone heat exchangers, it permits the integration of the cooling and 
heating of two process streams in the same heat exchanger, predicting whether the heat transfer between 
the two streams is feasible, and what heat exchanger area is required to complete the task. In terms of 
improving distillation columns, PRO II has a shortcut column function, which aids in determining the column 
design parameters required to complete a given separation and even indicate whether the separation can 
be accomplished.    
2.1.7 Step 2.2: New design alternative rigorous simulation 
Once new design alternatives have been established, the next step is to perform rigorous simulations of all 
the different alternative designs. Again the process simulator PRO II is used for this purpose and the 
required input data and output data that results from this step are as given earlier in Table 2-1. The main 
objective of the simulation is, as before, to obtain the mass and energy balances data, equipment duties 
and sizing parameters associated with each new design alternative, in order to perform a full sustainability 
analysis for each alternative.  
2.1.8 Step 2.3: Full sustainability analysis of new design alternative 
As previously described in steps 1.2-1.4, the information obtained from the simulations of the alternatives, 
is utilized to perform full sustainability analyzes of each new design alternative, as it initially was done for 
the base case. Again this will require the three software tools; SustainPro, ECON and LCSoft. The 
assessment results form a multi-criteria checklist, which holds all the necessary information in order to 
compare the alternative designs against each other and towards the base case. The data input and 
outcome from these analyzes are as given earlier in Table 2-2, Table 2-3 and Table 2-4. 
2.1.9 Step 3.1: Comparison of full sustainability results 
In this step the full sustainability analysis results for the base case design, are compared to the equivalent 
results obtained for all the new design alternatives. The energy demand, utility costs and carbon footprints 
of all the unit operations are compared against each other in a chart, in order to visually compare the 
==PAGE:20==
12 | P a g e  
 
extent of the variations. If a basis for comparison still exists, the SustainPro results are also compared. This 
will require that paths in the new alternatives corresponding to those identified for the base case exist.   
2.1.10 Step 3.2: Selection of best alternative 
In this last step, selection of the best design alternative, that best meets the set design target 
specifications, is chosen. Here a series of sustainability metrics on a per kg product basis are prepared for 
the base case and all design alternatives, in order to compare accurately, given that the production flow 
rate might vary. The most important metrics for each design are depicted in a radar plot, in order to overall 
visually compare the alternatives against each other and the base case design. When choosing the most 
important metrics to be compared in the radar plot, it should be remembered that the design targets 
should be taken into account, so the radar plot illustrates how the different designs satisfy these targets.   
2.2 Computer tools 
In the following, short descriptions of the computer tools used in the computer-aided framework are given.  
2.2.1 PRO II 
PRO II is a steady state process simulator that allows one to setup a chemical process, model the units 
involved and simulate the process in order to acquire stream information and conditions. It offers a wide 
variety of thermodynamic models and various unit operations to choose from. For each unit operation, PRO 
II requires a number of input specifications in order to model the equipment. The components included in 
the process must be defined, and for input streams, stream conditions, compositions and flow rates must 
be specified. Based on the input specifications and chosen models, PRO II is able to perform mass and 
energy balances and determine equipment duties and various chemical and physical parameters. 
In some cases PRO II can also calculate equipment sizing parameters. This holds for example for heat 
exchangers, where if the heating or cooling transfer medium and its inlet and outlet temperatures, as well 
as the overall heat transfer coefficient U are specified, PRO II performs the heat exchanger area calculation. 
For distillation columns, if the type of tray, tray spacing and flooding factor is specified, PRO II can estimate 
the column diameter. In the situation of designing a distillation column, PRO II also offers a short-cut 
column function. If provided with input stream specifications, required recoveries in the top and bottom 
streams, and a R/Rmin ratio, it can perform a minimum reflux ratio calculation, and for R/Rmin ratios in the 
region of the one specified, propose possible column designs that are able to perform the required 
separation. This includes the number of trays, optimal feed tray position and associated reboiler and 
condenser duties.  
==PAGE:21==
13 | P a g e  
 
The energy and mass balance data can be presented in a wide variety of different summary tables, from 
which component molar and mass rates, conditions, chemical and physical parameters and enthalpies for 
each stream can be acquired. In addition to this, a wide variety of equipment tables can be generated, from 
which equipment parameters and duties can be attained.  
2.2.2 SustainPro 
SustainPro is an excel-based software tool that incorporates the method of Carvalho et al. (2008) (3), which 
is able to perform a mass and energy balance indicators detailed analysis. SustainPro first requires general 
process data such as the number of streams, number of units, their types, the number of components and 
their molecular weight, the number of reactions and the annual operation time. Additional inputs regard 
mass and energy balance data, process stream conditions and enthalpies, and equipment duties. The mass 
and energy balance data obtained from PRO II is exported to an excel sheet which can be imported to 
SustainPro.  
In order for SustainPro to figure out how the process streams and units are connected and thereby through 
the stream data given, how the components flow through the process, it is necessary to specify for each 
stream, the unit from which the stream emerges and to which unit the stream is led to. For processes 
comprising reactive units, the heat of the reactions must also be specified.  
From the input specifications, SustainPro performs a flowsheet decomposition into open (OP) and closed 
cycle paths (CP) with respect to mass (MOP and MCP) and energy (EOP and ECP). A closed path means that 
a compound is being recycled, while an open path corresponds to a certain compounds route from entering 
and leaving the system. For each MOP and MCP, various mass and energy indicators are determined and 
given in an ordered list, highlighting process bottlenecks regarding resource loss and energy costs, which 
hold potential for improvement.  
By further specifying the reaction stoichiometric of the reactions involved, and the prices connected to the 
various demand streams, as well as the purchase prices connected to the various components of the feed 
streams, SustainPro determines a material value added (MVA) indicator for all MOP’s. This indicator holds 
information regarding the value generated between the entrance and exit of a certain compound in a given 
path. Hence if a negative MVA results, the compound has lost value, which is not a desired outcome and 
improvement is achieved by increasing this value.  
If the utility prices of high pressure steam, low pressure steam, cooling water and electricity are specified 
together with input regarding which of the above types of utility each duty is satisfied by, SustainPro can 
determine the energy waste cost (EWC) indicator. This also requires specification of pure component 
==PAGE:22==
14 | P a g e  
 
properties, including the density, heat capacity and heat of vaporization for all components in the process. 
These properties are temperature and/or pressure dependent, and hence have to be determined for the 
conditions of the input streams to the heat exchanging units. This can be done in ICAS. The EWC indicator 
contains information about the maximum theoretical amount of energy cost that can be saved in each 
mass open and closed path. A highly positive value hence means that the energy demand cost in this path is 
high and that the path therefore contains units with high operating costs.  
Once the MVA and EWC indicators have been determined the total value added (TVA) indicator can be 
found. It describes the economic influence that a compound has in a certain path, and is given by TVA = 
MVA – EWC. Thus if a highly negative MVA has been obtained, and the EWC value is small, a corresponding 
highly negative TVA value results. Another indicator that is determined is the accumulation factor (AF), 
which is applied only to the closed paths and describes the accumulative behavior of a compound in those 
paths. The remainder of the indicators SustainPro can determine are not taken into account in this project, 
and a description of these is therefore not given here.  
In the end SustainPro gives an ordered list of path indicators, placing those with the highest potential for 
improvement, which are those that have attained the highest negative (MVA and TVA) or positive (EWC, 
AF) values at the top. The indicators are given different colors in order to indicate how high their potential 
is, with red corresponding to a very high potential, blue to a high potential, light blue to an intermediate 
potential, green to a low potential and orange as being advantageous for the process. However just 
because an indicator presents a high potential for improvement, it does not automatically mean that it is a 
good indicator to change. Therefore a column showing the probability for improvement associated with 
each indicator value is also present. 
2.2.3 ECON 
ECON is an economic excel-based tool, that can perform a full economic evaluation of a chemical process, 
based a modular method, as given by Biegler et al. in Systematic Methods of Chemical Process Design (4). In 
order to do so ECON requires input regarding the annual raw material and product, or other income or 
expense flow rates, and their associated unit prices. Based on this, a profit margin is determined. ECON also 
offers the option to cost estimate the process equipment, including procedures for specifying various main 
equipment sizing parameters for a range of different types of equipment. In this work a chemical 
engineering plant cost index of 611 (5) and update factor of 1.565 was assumed, and specified to ECON.  
Input regarding the equipment duties can also be specified. Here ECON allows one to choose from high 
pressure steam at 254ºC, medium pressure steam at 184 ºC, and low pressure steam at 160 ºC for heating 
==PAGE:23==
15 | P a g e  
 
requirements, or cooling water for cooling demands. For some units, such as furnaces, a source of coal, 
natural gas or fuel oil can also be chosen. For electrical units, an electrical work load in kW can be specified. 
Dependent of the type of utility chosen, a utility cost estimate result, based on the utility costs specified in 
ECON. The standard prices used by ECON are given in Table 2-6 in section in section 2.3. Based on the 
estimated equipment purchase costs, utility costs and the profit margin, ECON performs a full economic 
evaluation, by first estimating the total capital investment (TCI) and operating costs. In this project inflation 
rates of 2%, a labor cost equal to 15% of the fixed capital investment, a US income tax rate of 39 % (6) and a 
land cost of 2% (7) of the TCI, have been assumed consistently.   
2.2.4 LCSoft 
LCSoft is a software that allows calculation of the carbon footprint of the process equipment as well as 
potential environmental impacts (PEI’s). In this thesis, only the carbon footprint results are utilized.  
As input LCSoft requires mass and energy balance data, stream conditions, and the equipment duties. In 
addition to this the product and raw materials must be defined through the wide range of component 
choices in the LCSoft database. The type of utility used to satisfy each energy demand must also be 
specified with respect to whether it is heating, cooling or electrical. Various types of heating, cooling and 
electrical utility types, based on their preparation method can be chosen. The process can also be split up 
into different sections by specifying input and outputs to each section. With respect to the inputs, this is 
both input streams and utility inputs to the units involved, while for the outputs, this regards waste 
streams, for which a compartment type can be specified.  
2.2.5 ICAS 
ICAS is an Integrated Computer Aided System consisting of a number of toolboxes that help to solve a wide 
range of problems. ICAS comprises extensive databases from which component information can be 
acquired. Standard properties are easily obtained, but ICAS also allows one to determine various 
temperature and/or pressure dependent properties by specifying the conditions. In this study ICAS is 
mainly used to determine the pure component properties, including densities, heat capacities and heat of 
vaporizations, that are required as input to SustainPro.  
2.3 Additional theory 
Some of the input-data needed in the computer-aided framework must be calculated manually. This applies 
to some of the sizing parameters required for estimating the purchase costs in ECON.

3 Conceptual example:  Cumene production process 
In order to test the computer-aided framework and illustrate and emphasize its advantages, the given 
methodology is first applied to a conceptual example. The conceptual example addresses a cumene 
production process which was developed by a group of students in a process design course for a conceptual 
process design project (16). The cumene process flow sheet from the design project is chosen as the base 
case design. The developed flowsheet was set up and simulated in PRO II in the design project and hence a 
PRO II file including this flowsheet already exists.  
3.1  Base case design 
The cumene process flow-sheet developed in the design project was based on literature about currently 
existing cumene production plants employing a molecular sieve alkylation technology, where propylene 
and benzene react through a Friedel-Crafts alkylation mechanism. The raw material feed stocks consist of 
polymer grade (P-grade) propylene which contains 5 % propane impurities, while the benzene feedstock is 
assumed to be pure. The base case flowsheet excludes the areas concerning feedstock handling, utility 
production or any product processing areas there might be. 
The main alkylation reaction that governs the production of the desired product is given by  (16; 17): 
    
            
      →     
       
In reality a number of unwanted side reactions take place. However in this design only the main side 
reaction has been taken into account, where a substantial amount of unwanted polyalkylate is formed due 
to a consecutive alkylation reaction taking place. The unwanted side reaction is a secondary alkylation, 
where the produced cumene reacts further with the propylene and results in the formation of 
diisopropylbenzene (DIPB) according to (16; 17): 
     
           
      →      
     
The secondary alkylation can give rise to different DIPB products, either ortho, meta or para, where ortho-
DIPB only is formed in only minor amounts, while the meta and para versions dominate, with the formation 
of p-DIPB often being slightly higher than m-DIPB (17). Based on this, the formation of o-DIPB is neglected, 
while both the p-DIPB and m-DIPB are assumed to be p-DIPB. This is reasonable, as both have similar 
boiling points, with p-DIPB having the highest (210ºC vs. 204ºC) (8).  
The formation of DIPB can be reversed by a transalkylation reaction according to (16; 17):

19 | P a g e  
 
      
     
    
       →        
       
The component molecular weights and standard boiling points of the 5 components included in the process 
are obtained from ICAS and are summarized in Table 3-1.

The cumene process flowsheet contains two reactors. In the first reactor, the alkylation reactor, the main 
alkylation reaction between propylene and benzene takes place producing cumene. The alkylation reactor 
is operated at 180ºC and 35 bar, with a benzene excess of approximately 3:1 relative to the propylene. 
These latter promotes complete conversion of the propylene and a selectivity of 82% towards cumene 
through the first reaction, while the remaining 18% reacts towards the side reaction (17). The transalkyation 
reaction is set to take place in the second reactor, the transalkylation reactor, reversing the cumene loss. 
The transalkylation reactor is operated at 150 ºC and 35 bar with a benzene excess of 10:1 relative to the 
DIPB. This is assumed to give full conversion of the DIPB back into cumene (17).  
A number of distillation columns are included downstream of the alkylation reactor in order to isolate the 
cumene product. The purpose of the first column (the depropanizer) is to remove the propane impurities 
present in the propylene feedstock, while the second column (the benzene recovery column) is present in 
order to recover the unutilized benzene, which is recycled. The third column (the cumene column) 
separates the cumene from the DIPB, which then is sent to be treated in the transalkylation reactor. The 
depropanizer column is operated at a high pressure due to propane’s very low boiling point at standard 
conditions. The outlet conditions from the alkylation reactor were kept as inlet conditions to the 
depropanizer column, avoiding the need to alter the conditions before entering the first column. For the 
benzene column, the separation is carried out at 1 bar and a feed temperature of 110 °C. The separation of 
the third column is completed at 1 bar and a feed temperature of 160 °C. The depropanizer and cumene 
columns both include 20 theoretical steps, while the benzene column contains 16. The feed trays of the 
tree columns are 3, 12 and 16 respectively. 
The cumene process flowsheet as it was established in PRO II in the design project is given below in Figure 
3-1. Mixers (Mi), splitters (SPi), heat exchangers (Ei), pumps (Pi) and a valve (V1) are included in order to mix 
and split streams and alter stream conditions to the correct inlet conditions. Pumps P1 and P2 both 
==PAGE:28==
20 | P a g e  
 
increase the inlet streams pressures to 35 bar, while E3, E4 and E5 heat the inlet streams to 160, 150 and 
180 °C respectively.   
To summarize the overall process depicted by the flow sheet seen in Figure 3-1, a stream (S1) containing 
benzene and propylene, in which benzene is present in an 3:1 excess, is fed to the alkylation reactor (R1), 
where the main alkylation reaction as well as the secondary alkylation reaction take place. The effluent 
from this reactor will largely consist of unreacted benzene, cumene and DIPB, which is sent to the first 
distillation column, the depropanizer (T2), which recovers the propane with the distillate. The eluate from 
this column is sent to the second distillation column, the benzene recovery column (T6), recovering the 
unreacted benzene, which as it can be seen, is recycled back to supply the two inlet streams to the 
reactors. The effluent from the benzene column is then processed through a third column, the cumene 
column (T4), which recovers the cumene with the lighter final product in S12, removing the DIPB with the 
eluate. This DIPB is then dissolved in a large excess of benzene supplied from the benzene recycle, before 
being fed to the transalkyation reactor (R2), where the transalkyation of DIPB back into cumene takes 
place. This reactor effluent is then mixed with the effluent from the depropanizer column, before fed to the 
benzene column, once again recovering the large excess of benzene from this reactor as well. Furthermore 
it should be noticed that a purge stream (S10) has been introduced.  
Based on the above, obvious errors to the design can already be pointed out. For one, a feedstock 
pretreatment and mixing section, that gives rise to the input stream S1, has been omitted. This section 
would have included units to mix both feedstocks and to heat and compress to the correct inlet conditions. 
Secondly the benzene and cumene column are operated at 1 bar, which is slightly under atmospheric 
pressure, which in theory would require vacuum. This aspect will be addressed when setting up a new 
design alternative, while the missing feedstock pretreatment section is just assumed not to have been 
taken into account.  
==PAGE:29==
21 | P a g e  
 
 
Figure 3-1. Cumene production flowsheet established in PRO II (16).
==PAGE:30==
22 | P a g e  
 
3.2  Part 1 – Process simulation, sustainability analysis and bottleneck 
identification 
In the previous section the base case cumene production flowsheet from the design project, was 
summarized. The first part of the computer-aided framework can now be applied to this flowsheet with the 
purpose of identifying potential bottlenecks in terms of sustainability, economic and environmental 
metrics.  
3.2.1 Step 1.1 –Base case rigorous simulation 
The rigorous simulation of the flow-sheet given in Figure 3-1 was performed in PRO II. The feedstock of mixed 
benzene and p-grade propylene is fed to the process at 79364.72 kg/hr; 62492 kg/hr (800 kmole/hr) 
benzene, 15990.8 kg/hr (380 kmole/hr) propylene and 881.94 kg/hr (20 kmole/hr) propane impurities.  
 The reactors R1 and R2 are modeled as isothermal conversion reactors for which the conversions 
mentioned previously have been specified. All three columns are equipped with both a column condenser 
and a reboiler, which in Figure 3-1 have numbers according to their theoretical step in the columns. In the 
following the condensers will be designated with a ‘c’ followed by the abbreviation of the column they are 
attached to. The same holds for the reboilers, with an ‘r’ instead. Hence the condenser and reboiler 
connected the depropanizer will be abbreviated by c-T2 and r-T2. The design specifications of the columns 
are given as desired recovery ratios of the key components in the top and bottom streams, and the reflux 
and thereby the duties of the condenser and reboiler are regulated in order to obtain the specified 
recoveries. However this does not hold for the cumene column, for which one design specification is 
regarding the recovery of the DIPB in the bottoms stream, while the other is regarding the reflux ratio that 
should be maintained at  1.4. The heat exchangers are modeled as countercurrent heat exchangers and the 
mixers have been given split ratios in order to obtain the desired ratios in the two reactors. The input 
specifications given to PRO II are summarized in Appendix Table A-1. 
The simulation mass and energy balance results obtained from PRO II are given in Appendix Table B-1. The 
PRO II calculated energy demands required by each unit in order to perform the specified task are seen in 
Table 3-2 and have been visualized in Figure 3-2.  
As it can be deduced from Appendix Table B-1, the benzene excess ratios are 3.13 and 10.11 in the inlet 
streams to R1 and R2 respectively. It can also be found that the feed of 380 kmole/hr propylene and 800 
kmole/hr benzene from S1, yields a final product stream (S12) with a purity of 98% in cumene, at a rate of 
approximately 387 kmole/hour = 46202 kg/hr.

Figure 3-2. Energy consumption associated with each unit operation. Blue = energy used for cooling, orange = energy used for 
electrical work and red = energy used for heating.  
 
Figure 3-2 shows that R1, c-T6, c-T4, P1, r-T2, r-T6, and r-T4 are found to be the highest energy requiring 
unit operations, with the alkylation reactor R1 being the largest. The high energy consumption of the 
alkylation reactor is due to the reactions being highly exothermic. In order to maintain the reaction 
temperature the reactor must be cooled with cooling water. The opposite holds for R2, which requires a bit 
of heating in order to maintain the reaction temperature, in which MP steam (184 ºC) was found to be 
sufficient.  
The heat exchangers E3 and E4 are to heat up the process inlet streams to 160 ºC and 150 ºC respectively 
and therefore also MP steam is sufficient as heating utility for both. E4 on the other hand must heat up the 
inlet stream to 180 ºC and therefore requires HP steam (254 ºC). T6 and T4 process relatively large 
amounts, as seen by consulting the mass balance data in Table B-1, which explains the high energy 
consumptions associated with their reboilers and condensers. The condenser connected to T2 on the other 
hand only requires a very small amount of energy, due to the very small amount of propane distillate that is

24 | P a g e  
 
released with S5. All three column condensers are to be cooled with cooling water and, while r-T6 and r-T4 
based on their output stream temperatures will require MP and HP steam respectively. From Appendix 
Table B-1 it is apparent, that the temperature of the cold stream leaving the depropanizer reboiler is above 
254 ºC and hence the energy demand of this reboiler cannot be satisfied by HP steam. In the design project 
(16) the chosen solution was to employ a fired reboiler supplied with fuel oil.  
The pumps will require electricity and both P1 and P2 increase the pressure of the inlet streams to a high 
pressure of 35 bar, explaining their high electrical work. As seen in Table B-1, P1 processes a stream 
approximately twice the mass of that which is led to P2, explaining why a higher work is associated with P1.  
As mentioned earlier, PRO II can conduct the heat exchanger area calculation for the standalone heat 
exchangers, if provided with the utility stream input and return temperatures, and the overall heat transfer 
coefficient. For E3 and E4, which both use MP steam, T1 = T2 = 184 °C, while for E5 which uses HP steam, T1 
= T2 = 254 °C. The process stream flowing through E3 will mainly consist of cumene and a smaller amount of 
DIPB. For the case of E4 and E5, the inlet streams are nearly pure benzene. Considering benzene and 
cumene as light to medium hydrocarbons, an overall heat transfer coefficient of U = 100 Btu/ft2·hr·ºF (18) (= 
567.8 W/m2·°C), for steam as the hot fluid and light to medium hydrocarbons as the cold fluid, can be 
assumed for all three heat exchangers. Based on this, PRO II estimated areas for E3, E4 and E5 which are as 
seen in Table 3-2. Also by providing PRO II with the tray type (Sieve trays) and a flooding factor of 80%, the 
distillation column diameters have also been estimated by as given in Table 3-2. 
From the above it can be found that the total energy demand is 223.54 GJ/hr, corresponding to an energy 
consumption of 0.0048 GJ/kg product. 99.1 GJ/hr is for heating tasks, 93.04 GJ/hr for cooling, and 31.40 for 
electrical work purposes.  
3.2.2 Step 1.2 – Mass and energy indicators analysis 
The base case design illustrated in Figure 3-1 is now to be evaluated in terms of mass and energy indicators 
by utilizing the excel-based tool SustainPro.  
From Figure 3-1 it can be seen, that a total of 16 units and 22 streams are involved in the flowsheet. The 
number of components present is only 5, and merely 3 reactions are involved. The annual operation time is 
assumed to consist of 300 working days of 24 hours equal to 7200 operating hours per year. The units were 
specified in SustainPro with the same abbreviations used in the flow sheet. With respect to the 
components, propane is specified as an inert (I), propene and benzene as raw materials (RM), cumene as a 
product (P) and DIPB as a byproduct (BP).  
==PAGE:33==
25 | P a g e  
 
The HMIS ratings found for each compound are summarized in Table 3-3. Based on these, propane and 
propene are found to be considered hazardous despite their slight health hazard rating. This is due to their 
flammability rating of 4 corresponding to them being very flammable gasses that may ignite spontaneously 
with air (19). Benzene is also found to be hazardous, with its health rating of 3, corresponding to major 
injuries, and furthermore the asterisk sign signifying that benzene constitutes a chronic health hazard. 
Secondly benzene has also been given a high flammability rating of 3, corresponding to compounds capable 
of ignition under almost all normal temperature conditions. Cumene is also found to be hazardous, due to 
its high flammability rating and it constituting a chronic hazard. DIPB is the only compound designated as 
not hazardous, since its health rating is only 2 which correspond to temporary or minor injuries, and its 
flammability is only considered to constitute a moderate hazard (19).  
Table 3-3. Summary of HMIS ratings for the 5 compounds present in the cumene process. Hazard scale: 0 = minimal hazard, 1 = 
slight hazard, 2 = moderate hazard, 3 = serious hazard, 4 = severe hazard, * = chronic hazard (19).

SustainPro is supplied with mass and energy balance data from Appendix Table B-1, the stream connections 
are specified and the reactor duties are given as the heat of reactions in both reactors. 
When determining the MVA’s, SustainPro detects three possible demand streams; S5, S10 and S12. The 
distillate from the depropanizer is not considered a side product, S10 is a purge stream, leaving only our 
cumene product demand stream S12, for which a selling price of 0.71 USD/lb (25) (=1.57 USD/kg) is specified. 
For the feed stream, purchase prices of 0.51 USD/lb (26) (=1.12 USD/kg) for P-grade propylene and 4.84 
USD/gal (25) (=1.46 USD/kg) for benzene are assumed.  
For the EWC indicator, the utilities prices for HP steam, LP steam, cooling water and electricity are specified 
according to the prices given in Table 2-6, and the heating or cooling duties connected to each piece of 
equipment are specified according to Table 4-2Table 3-2. For the case of the utilities specifications, it should 
be noted, that since only LP and HP steam are available options in SustainPro, the units using MP steam are 
defined as using HP steam. In addition to this, it was necessary to regard the fired reboiler as using HP 
steam.  
The pure component properties determined in ICAS as well as all the other input values specified to 
SustainPro can be seen in the SustainPro worksheet found on the attached CD-ROM at the back. 
==PAGE:34==
26 | P a g e  
 
The complete list of ordered indictors for the open and closed paths is seen in Appendix Table C-1. It is 
apparent, that most of the indicators are green, having low potential, while only a few (red) have been 
given a very high potential and one an intermediate potential. Most of those indicators with a very high 
potential have also been associated with a high probability.  
The paths connected with the 7 very high potential indicators are considered as potential bottlenecks with 
respect to material loss and energy costs. The path connected with the intermediate potential EWC value is 
also considered, seeing that it has a medium probability. These 8 mass open and closed paths identified by 
SustainPro are summarized in Table 3-4.  The same paths are visualized in Figure 3-3.  
The units included in the paths associated with high potential EWC values are considered possible hotspots 
with respect to energy costs. As it can be seen in Figure 3-3, all unit operations are included in these paths, 
with the exception of only the depropanizer column condenser c-T2.

Figure 3-3. Illustration of bottleneck paths identified by SustainPro. 
==PAGE:36==
28 | P a g e  
 
According to Figure 3-3, OP 5 is benzenes path from S1 till its exit from the system with S10. Due to the large 
flow rate of benzene in S10 and no selling price specified for this stream, SustainPro recognizes that a very 
large amount of benzene (32145.55 kg) is lost through the purge stream and loses its monetary value, 
resulting in a highly negative MVA value. Consequently a large negative value of the TVA indicator for this 
path is obtained, due to the value of EWC being relatively small. OP 6 is benzenes path from stream S1, 
through all three columns and out with the cumene product stream S12. An amount of 592.76 kg 
benzene/hr is removed with this stream, consequently also resulting in a relatively high negative MVA 
value, since only a selling price for the cumene is given for this stream. The benzene leaving with the 
cumene product stream is benzene that the benzene column was unable to recover, and a more efficient 
separation in the benzene column would therefore improve this problem and consequently result in a 
higher purity product stream.  
OP 10 is cumene's path from it is formed in R1 and leaves with the product stream S12, similar to OP 6. OP 
12 is cumene’s path from produced in R2 to its exit with S12, while OP 14 is DIPB’s path from its production 
in the alkylation reactor, passing through all three columns and recycled back to the transalkylation reactor, 
where it is consumed. In these three cases, the EWC indicator was associated with the high potential. These 
three paths therefore include unit operations that are responsible for the highest energy consumption cost. 
This is due to, as it was seen in Figure 3-3, that these three paths together include the three reboilers, E3, 
E4 and P1, which were seen in Figure 3-2 to be units associated with the highest heating and electrical duties.  
C1 is the cycle path for propane being circulated from the benzene column, leaving with S8 and sent 
through the transalkylation reactor and back to the benzene column. Propane is an inert which 
continuously enters this cycle path, accumulating the path due to more propane being added to the path 
than removed with S10. This yields a high AF indicator value. The same holds for C3, which is the same 
path, only for benzene. Benzene is continuously added in larger amounts to the cycle path than the amount 
removed with S10 and thereby accumulating in the cycle path. A high potential EWC was also identified for 
this path. This is due to the expected high utility cost of E4 and P1. Even though C1 is an equivalent path 
including the same unit operations, a much lower EWC vale was found, and this is due to the fact that the 
benzene flow rate of path C3 is much larger that the propane flow rate of C1. The cycle path C8 is benzenes 
path from the alkylation reactor, passing through the benzene column recycled back to the alkylation 
reactor. Here high potential values were found for both the EWC and AF indicators. The benzene 
accumulates in this cycle path because more benzene is added to the path from S1, than removed from the 
paths via S5 and S10 together. The very high EWC again indicates that this cycle path also contains unit 
==PAGE:37==
29 | P a g e  
 
operations with potential bottlenecks on the energy cost front. As it is seen this path contains P2 and E4 
and has high benzene flow rates, explaining the high EWC value.  
From Table 3-5 it is apparent that mostly benzene is the compound associated with the high potential paths. 
This is an expected outcome due to the large excess in which benzene is added to the process in order to 
obtain high conversions in the two reactors.  
Figure 3-3 shows that SustainPro has identified all unit operations, but c-T2 as potential bottlenecks with 
respect to energy. Besides this two paths were also found to be associated with high material loss. 
3.2.3 Step 1.3 – Full economic evaluation  
In this section, a full economic evaluation of the base case is performed through ECON, identifying process 
bottlenecks in terms of economic metrics. 
Common for the splitters, mixers and pumps, a sizing parameter required for costing these pieces of 
equipment, is the capacity in m3/s. For this, the inlet stream rates to these units from the mass balance are 
used. In the case of the heat exchangers, a sizing parameter required is the heat exchanger area. These 
were estimated by PRO II as given above in Table 3-2. Important sizing parameters for the reactors are the 
height and the diameter, two parameters that are estimated by assuming a holding time of 5 minutes and 
using equations ( 3 ), ( 4 ) and ( 5 ) given in section 2.3. The required inlet stream density can be obtained 
from PRO II’s stream summary table. For example, for R1, the output stream is S3, which has a flow rate of 
110631.02 kg/hr and a stream density of 685.996 kg/m3. Using equation ( 3 ), this yields a vessel volume of

assumed to be sieve trays, since these are normally the cheapest, and a tray spacing of 24 inches = 609.6 
mm and a flooding factor of 80% (4) are specified. The tray diameter can be taken to be slightly smaller than 
the column diameter. The height is determined using equation ( 6 ), for example for column T2:

the hot stream inlet and outlet temperatures are T1 = T2 = 184 °C, corresponding to MP steam. The process 
stream is the cold stream and the inlet and outlet temperatures are obtained from the distillation column 
temperature profile calculated by PRO II: t1 = 143.6 °C and t2 = 153.1 °C. A value of U = 100 Btu/ft2·hr·ºF is 
also assumed for the reboilers r-T6 and r-T4. This is since the cold process stream through these reboilers 
mainly consists of cumene and DIPB, with DIPB also assumed to be a medium hydrocarbon. For r-T6, the

In ECON the capital cost of r-T2 is estimated as a fired heater, which does not require a heat exchange area, 
and is only estimated based on the duty, pressure and type.  
For the condensers, the same equations are used, but this time the cold stream is the cooling water, with 
an inlet temperature of t1 = 20 °C and a return temperature of t2 = 30 °C. The hot stream temperatures are 
those of the process streams inlet and output. For c-T4, these temperatures are T1 = 151.3 °C and T2 = 147.8 
°C and a duty of 35.69 GJ/hr was determined by PRO II. For all the column condensers an overall heat 
exchanger transfer coefficient of U = 80 Btu/ft2·hr·ºF (18; 4) (= 454.24 W/m2·°C) was assumed, for the cold 
fluid being water and the hot fluid being low-boiling hydrocarbons. Propane, benzene and cumene 
respectively, are mainly present in the hot fluids passing through the condensers, and are all considered as 
low-boiling hydrocarbons. A calculation example for c-T4 yields an area of:

electricity, LP, MP and HP steam used in ECON, are the same as those given in Table 2-6. The utility costs

from the steam is accompanied by a phase change, therefore by using equation ( 7 ), the flow rates can be

some of the highest energy consumers. Instead, pumps P1 and P2 can be included in the most expensive.

constitutes a considerable percentage of the overall utility cost.  
Based on Figure 3-4 it can be concluded that P1, P2, r-T2, r-T6 and r-T4 are to be considered the process 
bottlenecks with respect to utility costs, identified by this analysis.  
For the full economic analysis, the quantities of the raw materials consumed, as well as the amount of the 
product produced, together with their expected unit prices, are also supplied to ECON. Based on these a 
profit margin is determined. This together with the estimated equipment capital and utility costs, ECON 
performs the economic evaluation. In Table 3-7 the annual raw material and product flow rates, raw material 
costs and product revenue as well as various economic metrics obtained are listed.

The outcome of the economic evaluation shows that the process is not at all profitable, with a negative 
payback time and a highly negative rate of return and net profit. This was of course expected, due to the 
==PAGE:42==
34 | P a g e  
 
profit margin being highly negative, as a result of the large benzene consumption, which besides the high 
benzene ratio utilized in the process, is increased even further by the large benzene loss through S10. In 
addition to this, the TCI would have been higher if the cost of the storage units and the feedstock 
pretreatment section units were taken into account, rendering the process even more non-profitable.  
3.2.4 Step 1.4 – Environmental impact analysis   
The excel-based software LCSoft is now used to evaluate and estimate the environmental metrics in the 
form of carbon footprints. A stream table containing mass balance data including stream names, stream 
conditions and component weight rates for each stream is established and imported to LCSoft. An 
equipment table is generated according to the obtained simulated duties. The product and product stream 
are defined as cumene and S12 respectively, and the raw materials benzene and propylene are found in the 
LCSoft chemicals database.  
The cooling water is assumed to be produced in an engine-driven cooler using natural gas. Hence the 
cooling water is represented by the type ‘Chilled water, engine-driven chiller using natural gas’, while the 
electricity was defined as ‘Electricity, natural gas, at power plant’. It is assumed that the steam is produced 
in an on-site industrial equipment combusting natural gas. Therefore for the units requiring steam, the 
energy source is defined as ‘Natural gas, combusted in industrial equipment’. For the fired reboiler, 
‘Residual fuel oil, combusted in industrial boiler’ was selected. Only one LCA section is generated for the 
entire process. Using the simulation results, the input stream was specified as the raw materials input 
stream S1, while the output streams were defined as S5 and S10.  
The carbon footprint results obtained are given in Table 3-8. These have been depicted in Figure 3-5. From 
this figure it can be seen that all unit operations only constitute relatively small carbon footprints, 
compared to r-T2. An overall carbon footprint of 0.36 kg CO2 equivalent has been determined.

The high energy consuming unit operations are expected to have high CO2 footprints and those associated 
with steam are assumed to be higher than those associated with cooling water.  
In the previous section it was found that R1, c-T6, c-T4, P1, r-T2, r-T6 and r-T4 were associated with the 
highest energy demands. When looking at Figure 3-5, it can be seen the expectations have been verified. 
The fired reboiler attached to T2 takes the number one spot, due to its usage of fuel oil, since the 
combustion of fuel oil leads to a high CO2 production, explaining why a much higher carbon footprint 
results, than if the equivalent amount of energy was satisfied by steam. The pumps have also been 
associated with high carbon footprints. P1 had and energy demand close to that of r-T4, and since the 
carbon footprint of P1 is much higher, it can be concluded that unit associated with electricity rating even 
higher than those associated with heating by steam.  
With respect to CO2 footprint, P1 and r-T2 can be considered the process bottlenecks identified in this 
analysis.

The results obtained by SustainPro are crosschecked with those found through the conducted economic 
and LCSoft analysis. A list of the unit operations according to their identification as process bottlenecks in 
each of the three analyzes is seen in Table 3-9. Those marked by SustainPro are those that were included in 
high potential EWC paths. Those recognized by ECON and LCSoft are those that were connected to the
