The highest EWC values were connected to OP 14 and C8. OP 14 includes all three reboilers as well as P1, 
while C8 includes only reboiler r-T2, but instead includes P2. These two thereby include all 5 unit 
operations identified with the highest utility costs, explaining the high EWC values obtained. An 
improvement of these will therefore also improve the later indicators.  
As we saw in section 3.2.2, SustainPro also identified highly negative MVA values connected to OP 5 and OP 
6, regarding the loss of benzene through S10 and S12, aspects that also must be taken into account. With 
respect to energy costs, the two global bottlenecks are obvious design targets in need of improvement. 
However the high utility costs of the two other reboilers and P1 should also be assessed.  
Based on the above, the main issues identified through all three analyzes are: 
 
Raw material (benzene) loss is high 
 
Utility costs are high in pumps  
 
Energy usage is high in distillation columns 
 
Utility costs are high for column reboilers 
 
Non-profitable process 
 
Carbon footprint is high for r-T2 and P1.  
Consequently design targets in order to overcome these issues would be to: 
 
Reduce the benzene loss significantly 
 
Decrease pump utility costs 
 
Decrease distillation columns duties 
==PAGE:45==
37 | P a g e  
 
 
Decrease distillation column reboilers operating costs 
 
Increase profit 
 
Decrease carbon footprint of r-T2  
 
Decrease carbon footprint of P1 
The aspect of raw material loss is addressed by means of mass integration. By reducing the benzene 
content in S10 and S12, the overall benzene loss can be brought down and consequently the MVA values 
identified for OP 5 and OP 6 can be increased. The reduction of benzene in S12 will also yield a purer 
product. The content in S10 can be significantly reduced by assessing the poor design of the purge stream. 
An alteration to the benzene recycle must be introduced in order to recover as much as possible of the 
benzene lost with S10. This would lead to a larger amount being recycled, and the feedstock benzene 
consumption could thereby be reduced. Further enhancing the separation efficiency in the benzene 
column, will allow less benzene to leave with the effluent and reduce the amount in the product stream 
S12. This would also further increase the benzene recycle load and allow further reductions of the benzene 
feed rate, overall leading to improvement of the economics of the process.  
Lowering the columns duties correspondingly will lower the connected utility costs and increase the overall 
profit of the process. Here focus lies on reducing the reboiler duties, since it was seen that it was these that 
were the main contributors to the overall column operating costs, as the price of steam highly exceeds that 
of cooling water. Therefore the reboiler duties are attempted lowered by assessing the designs of the three 
columns. If the separation specifications and the number of trays and feed trays are held constant, then it 
holds that the closer the inlet temperature is to the heavy key boiling point, the lower the reboiler duty, 
and conversely for the condenser duty, if closer to the light key component boiling point. However if only 
the number of trays is increased, the same separation efficiency can be obtained with a lower reflux ratio, 
resulting in lower condenser and reboiler duties. This most often means that extra capital cost investments 
must be sacrificed in order to reduce the annual operating costs and increase the overall profit. However 
the extra capital investment should not be too large to allow the operating cost savings to payback this 
extra capital investment within an acceptable amount of time.    
In addition to improving the economics and energy consumption of the process, the reduction of the 
column duties will also decrease the carbon footprint of the columns. r-T2 is associated with a high energy 
requirement and consequently a high carbon footprint. However since r-T2 is based on fuel oil, the carbon 
footprint of r-T2 is even higher, compared to that of units requiring equivalent heating demands. In 
addition, T2 is operated at a very high pressure, and it is assessed whether this pressure can be lowered. A 
pressure reduction would reduce the component boiling points and thereby reduce the temperature of the 
==PAGE:46==
38 | P a g e  
 
bottoms stream and might allow heating by regular steam. This would allow a significant reduction of its 
carbon footprint. The optimal would be to decrease the pressure by an extent that would allow a reduction 
of the bottoms stream temperature to below 250 ºC, rendering regular steam to be sufficient.  
With regard to the pumps, it is investigated whether another method for increasing the pressure that offers 
lower workloads, can be used instead of the pumps. A compressor is one alternative solution for this task. 
However these can only treat vapor streams. 
Based on the above, it is obvious, that the cumene base case design is a bad design, especially 
economically, as the process is highly unprofitable. This is mainly due to the bad design of the benzene 
recycle loop, which releases a large amount of benzene with the purge stream S10. In the following 
sections better and more sustainable alternative designs that meet the design targets are attempted found.  
3.3  Part 2 – Generation and screening of new design alternatives 
The base case has now been analyzed and has led to the identification of the high valuable material loss 
issues as well as the hotspots responsible for high energy costs and carbon footprints. Design targets in 
order to overcome these issues were stated previously and means to how these design targets are to be 
met have also been discussed. In the following, new alternatives are established, and full sustainability 
analyzes of the new alternatives are performed in order to compare them against the base case and to one 
another. 
3.3.1 Step 2.1 – Generation of new design alternatives 
3.3.1.1 New design alternative 1 
The objective with this first new design alternative (ND) is to decrease the benzene loss through S10. 
However first the minor errors regarding the operating pressures of the benzene and cumene columns of 1 
bar are corrected by altering these to 1 atm = 1.013 bar. This is done by adjusting the output pressure from 
V1. This does not result in any noticeable variations in the simulation results. In addition to this, the feed 
stock supplied by S1 is split into two feed streams, so S1 now only supplies the p-grade propylene, while a 
newly added stream supplies pure benzene at 35 bar and 180 ºC.  
The poor design of the benzene recycle includes a purge stream which releases an absurd amount of nearly 
pure benzene. Using the simulator, the design is altered by firstly removing splitter SP2 and consequently 
streams S4 and S10, and connecting SP1 directly to S8 in order to investigate whether the whole amount 
can be recycled directly. This required addition of a new mixer (M4) which mixes the recycled benzene with 
the new benzene feedstock stream. The output from M4 is then mixed via M1 with the propylene 
==PAGE:47==
39 | P a g e  
 
feedstock supplied by S1. The old purge stream previously removed approximately 400 kmole/hr benzene, 
while S1 supplied 800 kmole/hr. Therefore, with the removal of the purge, the benzene input is initially set 
to supply the process with 400 kmole/hr benzene. However in order to reduce the benzene supply to the 
minimum, yet still ensuring that the ratios in inlet streams to the two reactors are maintained, two 
controllers (CN1 and CN2) are added. CN1 is set to control the benzene/propylene ratio to be 
approximately 3 in S2 at all times, by adjusting the feed flow rate of pure benzene. CN2 is set to control the 
benzene/DIPB ratio to approximately 10 in S19 at all times, by adjusting the splitter specification of SP1.  
It was found that the entire amount could be recycled, without critical accumulations of other compounds 
resulting. The resultant new design alternative flow sheet is seen in Figure 3-6  
3.3.1.2 New design alternative 2 
The next aspect addressed in this second alternative, is the depropanizer column. The objective with this 
column is to decrease the column duties, most importantly the reboiler duty, and lower the bottoms 
stream temperature. The base case depropanizer is operated at 35 bar and an inlet temperature of 
approximately 181 ºC. At 35 bar, the two key components in the depropanizer, propane and benzene, have 
boiling points of 86.14 ºC (8) and 262.25 ºC (8). The possibility for operating the column at a lower pressure is 
investigated. A lower pressure would result in lower component boiling points reducing the top and bottom 
product stream temperatures and condenser and reboiler duties. A bottoms temperature that is below 254 
ºC would allow usage of a standard reboiler using steam instead of a fired reboiler using fuel oil. This would 
reduce the high carbon footprint for r-T2. Steam has a higher energy unit price than fuel, however if the 
reboiler duty is lowered significantly enough, the standard reboiler using steam can still be found to be 
more feasible in terms of utility cost.  
Using PRO II and the shortcut column function, the propane column is simulated at various pressures below 
35 bar. It was found that the lowest feasible pressure to operate the column at was 10 bar. At this pressure 
propane’s boiling point is 26.89 ºC (8) while benzene’s is 178.69 ºC (8). The bottoms stream from this column 
is mainly composed of benzene, and the temperature of this stream can therefore be expected to be 
around this temperature. Based on this, a pressure of 10 bar should allow us to use a reboiler using HP 
steam. In addition to this the heavy key component (benzene) boiling point will be closer to the inlet 
temperature to the column, decreasing the reboiler duty further. Again using a shortcut column in PRO II 
with specifications requiring 99.99% of the propane recovered in the top product and 99.99% of the 
benzene recovered in the bottoms stream, PRO II performs a minimum reflux ratio calculation for an inlet 
pressure of 10 bar. Here it was found that for the initial design of 20 stages, by changing the feed tray to 
tray 5, a much lower reboiler duty than that of the base case could be obtained. The initial amount of 
==PAGE:48==
40 | P a g e  
 
stages of the depropanizer is therefore kept, the feed stage altered and a valve (V2) inserted prior to the 
column, altering the inlet stream pressure to 10 bar. The possibility of increasing the number of trays in 
order to decrease the reboiler duty even further was also investigated, but it was found that this was not 
feasible, as many extra trays only gave a minimal further duty reduction. 
This new alternative flow sheet is given in Figure 3-7 
3.3.1.3 New design alternative 3 
In this design alternative, the aspect of reducing the duties of r-T6 and r-T4 are incorporated. The cumene 
column is now operated at 1.013 bar, and the inlet stream has a temperature of 160 ºC. At this pressure the 
light (cumene) and heavy (DIPB) key components boiling points are 152.32ºC (8) and 209.78 ºC (8). Hence the 
inlet temperature is nearer to the light keys boiling point, and the reboiler duty could be reduced by 
altering the inlet temperature. This would however require an increase of the duty of E3. It was 
investigated by simulation whether this was economically feasible. However it was found that the duty 
increase in E3 would result in a large extra utility cost that would be higher than that which could be saved 
by the associated reboiler duty reduction.  
The next thing investigated was the possibility to bring down the duties by increasing the number of trays 
in the column. Again a shortcut column in PRO II was utilized for this purpose. Requiring recoveries of 
99.9% of the cumene in the top stream and 99.9% of the DIPB in the bottom stream, it was found that 
significant condenser and reboiler duty reductions could be obtained if the number of theoretical steps 
were increased to 25, with a feed tray location at tray 18. Further increasing the amount of trays did not 
lead to further feasible reductions, and the cumene column design is altered to the latter.  
The objectives with regard to T6, are both to improve the separation efficiency in order to reduce the 
benzene loss with S12, as well as reduce the reboiler duty. The benzene column is operated at 1.013 bar 
and at this pressure the light key (benzene) and heavy key (cumene) component boiling points are 80.13 ºC 
(8) and  152.32 ºC (8) respectively. The base case cumene column inlet temperature was 110.53ºC, but as it 
shall be seen, the alteration of the depropanizer column pressure has already resulted in a reduction of this 
temperature to 94.55 ºC, and hence an increase in the reboiler duty. 
In order to enhance the separation efficiency of the column, a shortcut column in PRO II is again employed 
to investigate by simulation, what the outcome would be if the recovery specifications required 99.9% of 
the benzene and 99.9% of the cumene in the top and bottom streams respectively. This requirement of 
course increases the duties even further, if the initial number of stages is kept. Therefore asking the 
shortcut column to perform minimum reflux calculations, it was investigated whether an increase in stages 
==PAGE:49==
41 | P a g e  
 
could counteract the elevated reboiler duty. Here it was found that by increasing to 24 theoretical steps 
and a feed tray at tray 16, a further duty increase could more or less be avoided. Further increasing the 
number of trays did not result in any further significant reductions.  
The new alternative flow sheet is seen in Figure 3-8. 
3.3.1.4 New design alternative 4 
The main purpose with this last alternative is to incorporate a solution to the issue of the high utility costs 
of the pumps. Here the simulator tool is once again used to estimate the effect of using compressors in 
place of pumps. The mixed stream fed to E4 is a mixture of liquid and vapor, and hence the entire stream 
cannot just been passed through a condenser in place of P1. Instead P1 is moved to prior of the mixing in 
order to only increase the pressure of the incoming bottoms liquid stream from T4. The incoming benzene 
stream to M3 is on the other hand completely vaporized, and the pressure of this stream can be increased 
in a compressor. A compressor C1 is therefore added, and hence the two incoming streams are increased 
to 35 bar prior to mixing in M3. The heating of the entire mixed stream then follows in E4. 
In the case of the stream passing through E5, the entire stream is also vaporized and can be processed in a 
compressor. Therefore a compressor C2 is added prior to E5 and set to introduce an outlet pressure of 35 
bar. P2 can then be removed. By placing the compressors before the heat exchangers, the temperature 
increase associated with the compression that takes place in the compressors, due to the adiabatic 
operation of the latter, can be utilized to result in a decrease in the duties of the heat exchangers. Here it 
was found that the pressure rises in both compressors were accompanied by temperature rises to output 
temperatures exceeding those of the reactor operating temperatures. Hence, the duties of E4 and E5 are 
transformed to cooling duties which can be satisfied by cooling water.  
The incorporation if the above is seen in flow sheet in Figure 3-9, for this design alternative.

3.3.2 Step 2.2 – New design alternatives rigorous simulation 
The four new design alternative flowsheets in Figure 3-6 through Figure 3-9 are simulated in PRO II. The input 
specifications to PRO II are mostly still the same as in Table A-1, with changes mainly only being with respect 
to the recovery specifications in the distillation. The light and heavy key recovery specifications of T2 have 
been increased to 0.9999 in ND 2, while for the benzene and cumene columns to 0.999 in ND 3. In addition 
to this, for the last design alternative, since E4 and E5 now are cooled with cooling water, the heat 
exchanger inputs to PRO II for these two have been altered. The cold stream is now specified as the utility 
stream with t1 = 20 °C and t2 = 30 °C and the process stream as the hot stream. The overall heat exchanger 
transfer coefficient have been changed to U = 80 Btu/ft2·hr·ºF (18; 4) (= 454.24 W/m2·°C) for the cold fluid 
being water and the hot fluid being low-boiling hydrocarbons.  
The simulation mass and energy balance results obtained from PRO II for all four new design alternatives 
are given in Appendix Table B-1 through Table B-5. The calculated duties and estimated sizing parameters for 
each design alternative are gathered below in Table 3-10. The total annual energy demand and energy 
demand per kg product can are summarized in  
Table 3-11.

Consulting the mass balance data (Table B-1 through Table B-5) for stream S12 it can be seen that the product 
purity of approximately 98% is maintained until ND 3, where the product purity is increased to 99.6% due 
to the alterations of the cumene and benzene columns. Also for S5, it can be seen that the propane purity 
of S5 has increased to 96.03% in ND 1 from 95.7% in the base case. Continuing to ND 2, this is increased 
further to 99.6% due to the increased recovery specifications of T2. In consulting the data for streams S2 
and S19, it can also be ensured that for all design alternatives, the required ratios of 3 and 10 respectively, 
have been satisfied. 
In order to justify the feasibility of each alteration a few simple calculations regarding the significant 
changes are performed. From the above table, it is seen that the first design alternative increases the 
duties of P2 and E5 due to the increased flowrate recycled. This is estimated to incur extra utility costs of 
around 1.27 million USD/year. It will also include increased capital costs for these two units. Quick cost 
estimations in ECON found that this only will require 3371 USD. However the removal of the benzene loss 
through S10 saves 231475 tons benzene/year, corresponding to approximately 337.95 million USD/year. 
Hence overall, the changes are highly profitable. 
Taking the second alteration into account, the new duties of T2, and the reboiler being able to use HP 
steam, leads to an overall utility cost reduction of 815362 USD/year for T2, compared to the base case. 
However this is counteracted by an increase in the reboiler duty of r-T6, due to the lowered input 
temperature to T6. The duty of r-T6 has increased to 31.2 GJ/hr. The condenser duty has however been 
reduced to 12.03 GJ/hr. These new ND 2 T6 duties increase the benzene column utility costs by 
approximately 0.78 million USD/year. Comparing this utility cost increase to the utility cost savings of 0.82 
million USD/year achieved for T2, it can be seen that an overall savings is the result. The changes made in 
this alteration are therefore found to be overall profitable.  
Moving on to ND 3, the reduced duties of r-T4 and c-T4 allow utility cost savings of 0.87 million USD/year. 
In order to obtain these savings, the cumene column alteration required increasing the number of stages to 
25. However the column diameter estimated has reduced to 2.44 m as seen in Table 3-9. Quick cost 
estimations in ECON for such a column yielded a cost of 209745 USD. This actually results in a capital 
==PAGE:56==
48 | P a g e  
 
investment reduction of 13533 USD. In the case of the benzene column alteration, it has already been 
discussed that the saving achieved for T2 were higher than the accompanied extra utility costs for r-T6. 
However the alteration of T6 in ND 3 leads to slight further duty increases of c-T6 and r-T6. This results in 
additional utility costs of 0.12 million USD/year. However the enhanced efficiency of the benzene column 
results in a purer product stream of 99.6%, which reduces the benzene loss even further. Now only 112.67 
kg benzene/hr leaves with the product stream in comparison to the initial amount of 592.96 kg/hr. This 
further reduces the required benzene input, giving extra savings of 5.05 million USD/year on the benzene 
loss front. From this it is apparent that the savings achieved by the additional benzene loss reduction are 
much higher than the extra utility costs that result from the increased performance of T6. Taking the extra 
capital investment estimate of 46187 USD associated with the benzene column into account, the changes 
incorporated into this design alternative are also found to be highly profitable, allowing a rapid payback 
time of the extra capital investment. 
Lastly when looking at ND 4, the utility cost reduction obtained by the addition of C1 and C2 amount to 
around 3.13 million USD/year. This required an extra capital investment of 1.58 million USD. This amount 
requires a payback time of 6 months based on the utility cost savings. Based on the latter, the changes 
added to this alteration are also found to be lucrative.   
By comparing the economical aspect of the positive and negative results of each alteration, it was seen that 
overall positive outcomes were obtained. In the following, each alternative is analyzed in more detail in 
order to truly verify further and with regard to other sustainability aspects, whether the alternatives overall 
are better than the base case design.  
3.3.3 Step 2.3– Full sustainability analysis of  new design alternatives 
Based on the simulation results for the new alternative flow sheets, a full sustainability analysis is 
performed for each design alternative, in the same manner as it was done for the base case. The first 
analysis performed is the mass and energy indicators analysis. The new mass and energy balance data as 
well as the duties are supplied to SustainPro, and in the cases where the conditions have changed, the pure 
component properties are determined once more. The component purchase and sale prices as well as 
utility unit prices used in the base case are also assumed for the new design alternatives.  All the input 
values specified to SustainPro can be seen in the SustainPro worksheets on the attached CD-ROM. 
The complete ordered lists of metrics for all four new design alternatives are summarized in Appendix Table 
C-2 through Table C-5. The paths equivalent to those associated with the identified indicators in the base case 
are seen in Table 3-12.

Below in Table 3-14, the main economic metrics obtained from the full economic analysis in ECON are 
summarized. The same inflation rates, income tax and land cost values have been used, as for the base 
case. As it can be seen, when comparing with the base case (Table 3-7) the total purchase cost has decreased 
from the base case to ND 1. Comparing Table D-1 and Table D-2 it can be noticed that this is mainly due to the 
removal of SP2 which had a purchase cost of 38553 USD, and the addition of M4 which only has incurred a 
cost of 6181 USD. Consequently the TCI has also decreased. The annual energy consumption and utility cost 
have however increased, due to the increased duties of E5 and P2. However due to the elimination of the 
benzene loss through the purge, the operating cost has been decreased significantly allowing a profit to be 
acquired. A 24% rate of return and a net profit of 35.51 million USD have been achieved.  
==PAGE:59==
51 | P a g e  
 
Moving on to ND 2, it can be seen that the total purchase cost and hence the TCI have decreased even 
further, mainly due to the replacement of the fired reboiler, which had a purchase cost of around 0.9 
million USD, to a standard reboiler with a purchase cost of only 10874 USD. The reduced operating pressure 
of T2 has also resulted in a lower capital cost of T2. The energy consumption, utility costs and hence the 
operating costs have also decreased and the rate of return has increased significantly to 88% with a net 
profit 64.9 million.  
Taking ND 3 into account the purchase cost and TCI have increased slightly compared to ND 2 due to the 
increased purchase cost of the benzene column. The energy consumption, utility costs and operating cost 
have been reduced even further due to the duty reductions of the cumene column. The increased product 
purity and consequently decreased benzene consumption have rendered this design alternative even more 
profitable with a net profit of 68.20 million USD.  
Looking at ND 4, it can be seen that the purchase cost and TCI has increased significantly compared to ND 3 
due to the additions of the two compressors which required an extra capital investment of approximately 
1.6 million USD, compared to the initial costs of the two pumps summing up to only 28403 USD. It can also 
be seen that the energy consumption has increased slightly from ND 3 to ND 4, despite the fact that the 
overall demand from electrical work has been reduced. This is of course due to the increase of the duties of 
the E4 and E5. However since the latter now are cooling duties, together with the decreased utility costs of 
associated with electrical work, the overall utility cost and hence operating costs have decreased further, 
making ND 4 even more profitable with a net profit of around 80.8 million USD.

3.4 Part  3 – Comparison of base case design against new design alternatives 
In the previous section, the results obtained from the full sustainability analysis of the new design 
alternatives were presented. In the following, these are compared in more detail to one another and to 
those found for the base case.  
3.4.1 Step 3.1 – Comparison of  full sustainability results 
The resultant energy requirements for all the design alternatives from Table 3-10 have been depicted 
together with those of the base case in Figure 3-10. 
As it can be seen, the results connected to the two reactors have as expected not been altered. Secondly, 
regarding the results obtained for the pumps, it can be seen that the new benzene recycle design results in 
an increase in the work done by P2, which has increased to 5038.57 kW (18.14 GJ/hr) from 2674.73 kW 
(9.63 GJ/hr). The increase is due to the increased flow rate from the purge recovery, passing through this 
pump. P1 is not significantly affected as the purge recovery mainly only passes through the recycle section 
containing P2. The following two alterations do not affect the work of the two pumps noticeably, but when 
looking at ND 4 it can be seen that the replacement of the P2 with the C2 and the relocation of P1 and 
addition of C1 achieves a significant overall electrical work reduction. P1 has decreased significantly, due to 
the low flow rate that now passes through this pump. Comparing the summed duties of P1 and C1 to that 
of P1 in ND 3, a large reduction has been achieved.  The same is apparent when comparing the energy 
demand of P2 in ND 3 to that emerged for C2 in ND 4.  
==PAGE:61==
53 | P a g e  
 
For the case of E3 it can be seen that in going from the base case design to ND 1, its heating duty has 
decreased slightly. This is a consequence of the decreased flow rate through this heat exchanger, which has 
resulted from the alteration of the benzene recycle loop and the addition of the two controllers. This has 
led to a decreased benzene input, altering the composition of the input stream to T6 and consequently the 
resultant effluent stream. The alterations of the propane column do not affect E3, but in going from ND 2 
to ND 3, it can be seen that the duty has decreased slightly further. This is explained by the alteration of the 
benzene column, which with its increased separation efficiency has increased the recovery of benzene in 
the distillate, reducing the amount that leaves with the effluent stream and hence the flow rate through E3.  
When looking at E4 it can be seen that the results are similar in going from the base case design to N3, as 
the alterations done so far do not affect this part of the process. Changes only occur when ND 4 is taken 
into account, which obviously is due to the fact that E4 changes from a heater to a cooler requiring cooling 
water. This also results in a larger heat exchanger area. Since the ratio of benzene to DIPB in S19 must be 
kept at 10, the benzene recovered from the purge is passed through the section containing E5. This results 
in a flow rate which has almost doubled compared to the base case, which is seen as an equivalent duty 
increase of E5. The following alterations of first T2 and then T4 and T6 do not affect E5 substantially, and 
first with ND 4 the duty and hence the required area changes, similar to E4, due to the shift from a heater 
to a cooler.  
For T2, in going from the base case to ND 1 a slight variation in the duty of r-T2 are seen. As mentioned 
previously, the alteration of the benzene recycle results in slightly changed composition of S2 and hence of 
the input stream to T2, consequently altering the recoveries of the column and hence the duties. 
Proceeding to ND 2, the reboiler duty is reduced substantially further to 5.70 GJ/hr while the condenser 
duty has increased to 1.97 GJ/hr. This is the result of the lowered pressure and hence component boiling 
points and column temperatures, as well as the feed tray shifting closer towards the reboiler. The lower 
component boiling points have also allowed an exit temperature from r-T2 of 190.18 ºC, and HP steam is 
now sufficient as heating medium. In addition to this, in Table 3-2 it was seen that a smaller column diameter 
also resulted.  
In the case of T6, it can be seen that for ND 1, the column duties have only been altered slightly, again 
being a result of the altered stream flow rates and compositions into the column which have resulted from 
the new benzene recycle. However when looking at ND 2, it can be seen that the reboiler duty has 
increased significantly to 31.20 GJ/hr, while the condenser duty has decreased substantially to 12.03 GJ/hr. 
These duty variations are due to the decrease in the temperature of the effluent stream from T2, lowering 
the inlet temperature of S25 to T6. In ND 3 the reboiler duty has increased slightly further due to the 
==PAGE:62==
54 | P a g e  
 
enhanced separation efficiency of the benzene column, which otherwise would have been much larger, if 
an extra amount of trays hadn’t been introduced to counteract this effect. With respect to T4, noticeable 
duty variations only occur with ND 3, where the redesign of T4 results in rather large reductions in both the 
reboiler and condenser duties. These redesign of T4 also resulted in a reduced column diameter, compared 
to the base case, as seen previously in Table 3-10.  
From the above it is apparent that the duty of r-T2 has been lowered significantly, however on the expense 
of that of r-T6. Table 3-16 shows the total energy demand of all three reboilers as well as the total energy 
demand for electrical work, in all designs. Here it can be seen that compared to the base case, overall 
reductions have been obtained for ND 2, ND 3 and ND 4 in terms of the reboilers, with the lowest energy 
requirement found for solution ND 3 which is only slightly better than ND 4. This offset is assumed only due 
to simulation result variations, as the replacement of the pumps with the compressors shouldn’t affect the 
energy requirements of the reboilers. For the case of the electrical work, only ND 4 yields an improvement.  
Table 3-16. Total energy demand of reboilers and total energy demand for electrical work comparison.

Next, the SustainPro results are compared. By comparing the paths in Table 3-12 with those of the base case 
in Table 3-4 it can be seen that OP 6 from the base case is the same path as OP 5 in all the new design 
alternatives, representing the loss of benzene with S12. The base case OP 5 has been eliminated by the 
removal of the purge stream, also eliminating the associated highly negative MVA value. The base case OP 
10 corresponds to OP 8 of ND 1 and OP 7 of all the remaining new design alternatives. For the base case OP 
12, it can be seen that the ND 1 OP 9 is equivalent, while for the rest of  the new design alternatives, the 
equivalent path is OP 8. For the base case OP 14, the ND 1 OP 11 is the equivalent, while for the rest, and 
OP 10 is the case. With respect to the cycle streams C1, C3 and C8 are equivalent in all design. Table 3-17 
summarizes and compares the values of the different sustainability indicators in the equivalent paths.

Comparing the MVA’s in the first row it can be seen that the MVA value has become less negative in all 
alternatives. In going from the BC to ND 1, a small improvement is achieved due to the new benzene 
recycle establishment with controllers reducing the benzene input to the process, hence reducing the 
amount of benzene in S12. The next change in this value happens after the alteration of the benzene 
column, improving the value significantly. As the TVA value is directly dependent of the MVA value, the 
same holds for the development of the TVA values.  
Next the EWC values in the third row are compared. These paths include r-T2, r-T6, E3 and c-T2. As it can be 
seen, a noticeable reduction is first obtained in ND 3, which is the result of the duty reduction of E3 and c-
T2. The reason why the significant reduction in utility cost of r-T2 obtained in ND 2 does not show in the 
EWC value for this design, is due to the equalizing associated utility cost increase of r-T6 being. Comparing 
the EWC values in the following row, it can be seen that a noticeable reduction is also only obtained in ND 
3. These EWC values include r-T6, E3 and c-T2, and hence the same holds for this path as the previous one.  
Regarding the results for the EWC values in the 5th row, it is apparent that from the BC to ND 1, a small 
reduction is obtained due to benzene input reduction, but a significant reduction is only obtained in ND 4. 
These EWC values include all 3 reboilers, E4 and P1. The large reduction can partially be explained by the 
fact that the utility cost associated with E4 is reduced significantly in ND 4. The main reason however, is 
due to the fact that the pressure increase now is performed prior to the mixer by P1 and C1. Hence the 
energy demand required for increasing the pressure of the recycled benzene to this path is already 
performed by C1 prior to the addition to the cycle path and therefore no longer affects the EWC value of 
this path.  
C1 and C3 are the same paths, only for propane and benzene respectively. They both contain c-T6, E4 and 
P1. Due to the increased flow rate of this path, seeing that more propane is being recycled due to the 
removal of the purge stream, the C1 AF value has increased from the BC to ND 1. Regarding C3, a significant 
reduction is only seen in the EWC value for ND 4, of course due to the reduction of the electrical work due 
==PAGE:65==
57 | P a g e  
 
to the removal of P1 and addition of C1 which offered a lower duty. The AF values for C3 are rather 
constant throughout all alternatives, due to the presence of the controller adjusting the benzene input.  
The cycle path C8 contains r-T2, c-T6, E5 and P2. Here a large increase is seen from BC to ND 1. This is a 
result of the increased duty of E5 and P2 due to the recycle alteration. A large reduction is also obtained in 
ND 4 due, again due to the reduction of both E5 and the electrical work performed in this path due to the 
substitution of P2 with C2. The AF value for C8 increases from the BC to ND 1, due to the recovered purge 
amount being added directly to this cycle stream.  
To further emphasize and support the SustainPro results regarding the benzene loss, Figure 3-11 illustrates 
the benzene consumption of all design alternatives. Here it can be seen that due to the establishment of 
the benzene recycle loop, the benzene consumption has been reduced significantly compared to what was 
fed to the base case. The positive effect on the overall benzene requirement that results from the 
alteration of the benzene column, which was introduced in ND 3, is also seen in the further slight reduction 
obtained in ND 3 and ND 4, which yields a further savings of approximately 4.2 million USD/year.

discussed previously. In all cases the reduction of the benzene consumption yielded a larger profit margin, 
which allowed the estimated operating costs to be taken into account and still yield a profit.

each unit operations carbon footprint, correspond to their energy consumption changes. This is however 
with the exception of r-T2, where it most importantly should be noticed, that after replacing the fired 
reboiler with a normal reboiler supplied with HP steam, the carbon footprint of r-T2 was reduced 
significantly. The removal of P2 and addition of C1 and C2 have also consequently led to an overall decrease 
in the carbon footprint associated with electrical work.

3.4.2 Step 3.2 – Selection of best alternative 
From the above, it is obvious that improvements have been made. But in order to better compare each 
design overall, a wide range of sustainability metrics are summarized for each case below in Table 3-19. Here 
it can be seen that a column containing the calculated standard deviations between the results of the base 
case and ND 4 are given. The deviation is calculated based on the base case value as the denominator, and 
hence a positive deviation means that a reduction has been achieved in going from the base case to the 
new design value. Opposite, a negative deviation means an increase has happened. In most cases, a 
decrease is the desired outcome, and hence a positive deviation is desired.  
As it can be seen in Table 3-19, most of the deviations of the variables in which a decrease is beneficial, a 
positive deviation has been obtained. A negative deviation has been found for the product purity, which is 
one of the cases, in which an increase actually is an improvement and hence a negative deviation is desired. 
In addition to this, it can be seen that a negative deviation also has been determined for the TCI, which in 
most cases is a required outcome in order to reduce the utility costs. The energy requirement and utility 
cost associated with cooling are also found to be negative, since an increase has resulted, due to E4 and 
E5’s switch to cooling units. A decrease would of course have been the optimal, but the increase in cooling 
energy consequently yielded an overall utility cost reduction, and hence may be perceived as beneficial. 
Lastly, it can be seen that for the rate of return, net profit and inverse net profit, a deviation has not been 
shown, as positive deviations are obtained in all cases due to the negative values of the base case. This will 
indicate a decrease, which is untrue. The net profit and rate of return are also two aspects, where an 
increase and hence negative deviation is desired. 
In order to illustrate the comparison of some of the most important variables from Table 3-19 with respect to 
the set design targets, percentage comparisons are prepared as seen in Table 3-20. A radar plot visualizing 
these, are seen in Figure 3-14. Here it should be noted that the otherwise important metric 1/Net profit has 
been omitted. This is due to the fact that it does not make sense to determine how much a percentage a 
positive number is of a negative number.  Here it should just be remembered, that it was found that the 
profitability of the new designs increase throughout ND 1 to ND 4, with ND 4 being the most profitable.  
In order for the new design to be more sustainable without any tradeoffs, the new design should lie within 
the boundary of the base case design in the radar plot. With respect to ND 1 this is not the case for 5 of the 
metrics. With ND 2 this has improved, to only be the case for 4 of the metrics, while for ND 3, only three 
remand. With the incorporation of the last aspects to ND 4, these are met, and hence ND 4 is the only non-
trade off more sustainable solution, meeting all the design target specifications. Compared to the other 
==PAGE:70==
62 | P a g e  
 
alternatives, ND 4 has achieved the best improvements with respect to almost all sustainability metrics 
taken into account, being the alternative that incorporates all of the changes. Hence ND 4 is found to be 
the best alternative, offering a much better process economy and a high net profit, based on a highly 
reduced benzene loss, lowered utility cost and carbon footprints of both global bottlenecks, as well as 
reduced operating costs of other high cost units, including r-T4 and P2, which has been substituted by C2.

Further alterations could have been done, which could further improve the process and make it even more 
sustainable. One aspect could be to use a cheaper propylene product which contains more propane, for 
example r-grade (refinery grade) propylene, which contains up to 50% propane. This would however 
increase the operational costs of the propane column as well as require a different column design. However 
the operational cost increase would be expected to mainly be connected to the column condenser, and 
might therefore be expected not to be to high, compared to the savings that could be achieved with 
respect to a lower raw materials cost. The flowrate of S5 would increase considerably and might be able to 
constitute an extra income, either sold or utilized for combustion for steam production.  
In this conceptual example the aspect of mass integration was of high focus, while energy savings were 
obtained by retrofitting the units. The aspect of heat integration was not addressed, even though the 
process holds opportunities for this aspect. For example, the heat from the exothermic alkylation reactions 
could be exploited elsewhere in the process by cooling R1 with a process stream in need of heating. Heat 
integration between the now cooling heat exchangers E4 and E5 and the heating heat exchanger E3 could 
also have improved the overall energy demand. If the scope of this conceptual design had been increased 
to include heat integration, an even more sustainable solution could most probably have been proposed.

4 Case study 1: HDA process 
As it was seen previously, the computer-aided framework employed to the conceptual example resulted in 
the establishment of various new design alternatives, and one of them were found to be a more 
sustainable solution without any trade-offs with regard to the target designs. The conceptual example base 
case design contained many opportunities for improving the design, as there were many poor design 
decisions which gave many aspects to be addressed. The next question is therefore, if the computer-aided 
framework may be utilized onto a process design, which already previously has been improved in one or 
more ways, and yet still identify hotspots holding possible potential to which additional improvements can 
be done.  
For the first case study a well-known and well-studied process, to which improvements already have been 
introduced, has been chosen. This is the published hydrodealkylation (HDA) of toluene to benzene process. 
The chosen base case design is a version of the traditional HDA process, to which the addition of a recycle 
employing a membrane has been established, in order to improve the previous issue concerning raw 
material (hydrogen) loss through a purge stream. The developed flowsheet has already been set up and 
simulated in PRO II by another party. 
4.1 Base case design 
The base case design is a newly improved version of the well-known traditional hydrodealkylation of 
toluene to benzene, designed by Douglas (27). It includes the reaction of toluene with hydrogen at high 
pressure and temperature. The gaseous hydrogen feedstock used contains 3% methane, while the liquid 
toluene feedstock is assumed to be pure. The base case flowsheet once again omits the areas concerning 
feedstock handling, utility production or any product processing areas there might be. 
The main product producing reaction is given by (28; 29; 30): 
    
        
  
        →
    
        
   
        
Unfortunately the produced benzene can result in another side reaction taking place causing formation of 
biphenyl and even more methane: (28; 29; 30) 
      
       →
      
         
   
        
The component molecular weights and standard boiling points of the 5 components are summarized in 
Table 4-1. 
 
==PAGE:73==
65 | P a g e  
 
Table 4-1. Process component standard properties (8).

The process includes one reactor in which both reactions take place at 600 K and 40 atm. An excess of 
hydrogen, of minimum 5:1, to toluene is necessary to prevent coking in the reactor (28; 29; 30). These 
conditions give rise to a toluene conversion of 75% (30) towards the main reaction, while it is the case that 
5% (30) of the produced benzene is consumed for the side reaction. Both reactions are exothermic.   
A flash is included in order to remove most of the produced methane and excess H2, and is operated at 40 
atm and 305 K. Besides this, three distillation columns are included downstream of the reactor. The first 
column has the purpose of removing most of the remaining H2 and methane, which could not be removed 
by the flash. The effluent from the flash hence mainly contains benzene and smaller amounts of toluene 
and biphenyl. This is sent through the second column, which has the purpose of recovering the benzene 
with the distillate and the toluene and biphenyl with the effluent. The last distillation columns purpose is 
then to recover the toluene with the distillate, for recycling back to the reactor, while the diphenyl is 
recovered in the effluent stream. Due to the low standard boiling points of H2 and methane, the first 
column is operated at 10.2 atm. The two last columns are operated at atmospheric pressure. The inlet 
stream temperature to the first column is 305 K, while the input stream temperature to the second column 
is 363.27 K. The effluent stream which is sent directly to the third column without any conditioning has a 
temperature of 385.69 K. Both the first and last columns consist of 10 theoretical steps, including a reboiler 
and a condenser, and both have a feed tray position at stage 4. The second column comprises 32 
theoretical steps with the feed tray at stage 20.  
The additional feature of this given design, lacking from the initial design by Douglas (27), is the presence of a 
membrane, introduced based on the results given by Luyben et al. (30) and Fischer et al. (28). The purpose of 
the membrane is to recover some of the hydrogen that previously was lost through a purge stream 
following the flash unit, allowing a reduction of the hydrogen feed rate. The membrane is operated at a 
permeate pressure of 9 atm.  
The HDA process flowsheet as it was established in PRO II is seen in Figure 4-1. In addition to the above 
mentioned units, a mixer (M2) and a splitter (SP1) are included in order to mix the preheated feed streams 
and recycle streams. Four heat exchangers (E1, E2, E3 and E4) also exist with the purpose of heating the 
input streams to the reaction temperature of 600 K, before mixing and input to the reactor. A valve (V1) is 
==PAGE:74==
66 | P a g e  
 
present in order to increase the pressure to the operating pressure of the first column, while a second valve 
(V2) is present after the column to bring the stream back to atmospheric pressure.  
To summarize the overall process depicted by the flow sheet seen in Figure 4-1, the two feed streams, S1 
and S2, supplying hydrogen and toluene respectively, are preheated to 600 K in E3 and E4, before mixed in 
M2 together with 3 recycle streams. The mixed stream is then sent to the reactor R1 and the resultant 
reactor effluent is processed in a flash unit F1, allowing some of the methane and H2 to flash. The flashed 
stream is led to splitter SP1, which splits the stream into a stream containing a manageable amount for the 
membrane, and a recycle stream which recovers part of the hydrogen alongside with some methane, which 
is recycled back to M2 and along the way reheated to 600 K in E1. The stream S13 is sent to the membrane 
(SC1), and the compressor C2 is present in order to recycle the permeate stream, altering the output 
pressure from the compressor back to 40 atm. The retentate from the membrane is removed and used as 
fuel for on-site furnaces and boilers. The effluent from the flash is passed through valve V1, which increases 
the pressure to 10.2 bar as required for column T1. The distillate from this column is also taken as fuel for a 
furnace or boiler. The effluent from T1 is brought back to atmospheric pressure via valve V2 before 
entering T2, from which the benzene product stream is obtained from the distillate. The effluent from T2 is 
sent directly to T3, which recovers the toluene with the top stream and recycles this back via a compressor 
C2, which alters the conditions of the stream to the reaction conditions of 40 atm and 600 K. The effluent is 
removed from the process with S20.  
All the heat exchangers are to heat up the process inlet streams to 600 K (=326.85 ºC). This temperature is 
not one that can be attained by using steam. Here a heat transfer fluid known as Dowtherm A, which is a 
eutectic mixture of biphenyl and diphenyl oxide is used. Dowtherm A fluid has a normal application range 
from 15ºC to 400ºC, and a pressure range from atmospheric pressure to 10.6 bar (31).  
==PAGE:75==
67 | P a g e  
 
 
Figure 4-1. HDA BC process flowsheet established in PRO II. 
==PAGE:76==
68 | P a g e  
 
 
4.2 Part 1 – Process simulation, sustainability analysis and bottleneck 
identification 
In the same manner as for the conceptual example, part 1 of the framework is executed in order to 
simulate and perform a full sustainability analysis of the base case.  
4.2.1 Step 1.1 –Base case rigorous simulation 
The methane-containing hydrogen feedstock is fed at a rate of approximate 182 kmole/hr (443.21 kg/hr), 
while the pure toluene is supplied at 135.9 kmole/hr (12521.9 kg/hr). 
The reactor R1 is modeled as an isothermal conversion reactor, and since the reactions are exothermic, 
cooling is required. All four heat exchangers are modeled as countercurrent heat exchangers and the three 
columns are all equipped with both a column condenser and reboiler. A stream calculator has been used in 
order to mimic the membrane unit. From Figure 4-1 it can be seen, that besides the process units, a 
controller (CN1), a calculator (CA1) and two optimizers (OP1 and OP2) exists. The purpose of CN1 is to 
control the required 5:1 H2/toluene ratio in the reactor feed stream, which is maintained by adjusting the 
flow rate of S1. In this manner the H2 consumption is adjusted to a minimum. The target of the calculator is 
to perform the membrane calculations, and these results are connected to the stream calculator in order 
for the stream calculator to separate the input stream according to the recoveries that would be obtained 
by the membrane. The two optimizer are included merely to optimize the feed tray position of T2 and T3, 
ensuring that the position of the latter always is most beneficial with respect to the column reboiler duty. 
Here it should be noted that the flowsheet depicts a feed tray at stage 15 for T2. However due to the 
presence of the optimizer, which has determined an optimal feed stage at stage 20, the simulation results 
for this column are determined based on this feed stage at not the one depicted on the flowsheet.  
The input specifications given to PRO II are summarized in Appendix Table A-2. 
The simulation mass and energy balance results acquired from PRO II are given in Appendix Table B-6, while 
the unit duties are seen below in Table 4-2. These have been visualized in Figure 4-2. From the mass balance 
data it can be realized that the hydrogen excess ratio is 5.01 in the inlet stream to R1. It can also be seen 
that the hydrogen feed rate of 176.43 kmole/hr together with the toluene feed rate of 135.9 kmole/hr, 
yield a final benzene product stream (S15) of 126.51 kmole/hr (9885.06 kg/hr) at a purity of 99.82 mole%.

The standalone heat exchanger areas calculated by PRO II are also given in Table 4-2. The areas calculated for 
E1, E2 and E3 are based on a Dowtherm steam temperature specification of T1 = T2 = 623.15 K and an 
overall heat transfer coefficient of U = 20 Btu/ft2·hr·ºF (18) (=0.11 kW/m2·°C), which can be assumed in 
situations Dowtherm is the hot fluid and gas is the cold fluid, which is the case since the cold process 
stream into these heat exchangers are vapor streams, mainly consisting of H2 and CH3. In the case of E4, the 
cold process stream is toluene, and the calculated area is based on a U-value of 15 Btu/ft2·hr·ºF (32) (=0.085 
kW/m2·°C). For the columns diameters, PRO II was supplied with sieve trays as the tray type and a flooding 
factor of 80%.  
From Figure 4-2 it is obvious that R1, F1, c-T2, E1, E4, r-T1 and r-T2 are found to be the highest energy 
requiring unit operations, with the flash being the highest. The reactions taking place in R1 are exothermic, 
resulting in the need for cooling of the reactor with cooling water in order to maintain the reaction 
temperature. The large process stream entering the flash is approximately 607 K, and is cooled to the flash

By multiplying the actual kg/hr flow rates of each component in each stream with given prices, an average

values that don’t take into account, the price associated with heating with Dowtherm. These heating duties 
are considered at the price of HP steam in the analysis. All the input values specified to SustainPro can be 
found in the corresponding SustainPro worksheet found on the CD-ROM.

the process further exist. The paths connected with all the very high and high potential indicators are 
considered, but also the path associated with the intermediate OP 15 EWC indicator is taken into account,

According to Figure 4-3, OP 3 is H2’s path from its input with stream S1 till it is consumed in the reactor. For 
this path the EWC value was found as a high potential indicator, and E3 is responsible for this. OP 6, which 
is methane’s path, from produced in the reactor till it is removed from the process with S18. Here a high 
potential MVA, and due to the low EWC value, consequently a high potential TVA value were determined. 
This is due to SustainPro recognizing that a large amount of methane is removed with this stream for which 
only a small fuel credit is obtained.  
OP 12 is toluene’s path from its input to the process through S2 till it is consumed in the reactor. Here the 
highest potential EWC value has been obtained, due to the large flow rate and E4, which as we saw in Figure 
4-2 had one of the highest heating duties.  
OP 15 is benzenes path from produced in the reactor, passing through the flash, T1 and T2 and leaving T2 
with the distillate. For this path only an intermediate EWC value has been obtained, due to this path mainly 
only containing cooling duty units and r-T1, which compared to E1 and E2 has only a small heating duty.  
Cycle paths C1 and C2 are equivalent paths, for H2 and methane respectively, including the reactor, flash 
and E1. High EWC values have of course been found for these two since the both include relatively large 
flow rates and pass through the number one energy requiring unit E1. High potential AF values have also 
been found for these paths. This due to the constant addition of hydrogen and methane to this cycle path 
from S3, S22 and S23, which is larger than the amounts removed from the path via S7 and S13, hence 
leading to accumulation. C8 which contains both r-T1 and r-T2 consequently also has been associated with 
a high potential EWC value. 
 
 
==PAGE:82==
74 | P a g e  
 
 
In Figure 4-3 it can be seen that all unit operations but E2, C2, c-T1, and r-T3 are included in the paths, and 
are identified as possible bottlenecks with respect to energy cost found from this analysis. With respect to 
material loss, the loss of methane through S18 is the only aspect identified by SustainPro.  
4.2.3 Step 1.3 – Full economic evaluation 
Next the full economic evaluation is performed.  The rest of the equipment sizing parameters are calculated 
manually as demonstrated earlier in the conceptual example. For the case of the compressors, which 
weren’t covered previously, the only required sizing parameters to ECON is the pressure, material and duty. 
The pressures for both compressors were specified to 4053 kPa = 40 atm and carbon steel is chosen as the 
material.  
Regarding the column reboiler area calculations, the previously used overall transfer coefficient of U = 100 
Btu/ft2·hr·ºF has been assumed, since the hot utility stream in all is steam and the cold process streams 
flowing through these reboilers mainly consist of benzene, toluene and biphenyl respectively, all assumed 
to be validly classified as light to medium hydrocarbons.  
For c-T2 and c-T3 the U-value of 80 Btu/ft2·hr·ºF is used again, since the cold fluids are water and the hot 
fluids in these two condenser (benzene and toluene respectively) also can be considered low-boiling 
hydrocarbons. With respect to c-T1, a U value of 3 Btu/ft2·hr·ºF (18) (= 0.017 kW/m2·°C) for the cold fluid 
being water and the hot fluid being a gas, is assumed.  
It is not possible to estimate a cost for the membrane in ECON. Here it is assumed that the membrane can 
be taken to have a capital cost of 126000 USD, as estimated for a 10-cell counter-current polymer 
membrane with an area of 229 m2 and permeate pressure of 9.5 bar, which was the proposed solution 
given in the journal article by G.R. Bouton and W.L. Luyben (30), to recover the hydrogen lost with the purge 
stream in the traditional HDA process. All the sizing parameters and ECON inputs as well as the resultant 
equipment capital costs are summarized in Appendix Table D-6.  
Since ECON does not include Dowtherm as a utility choice, the utility costs associated with the 4 stand-
alone heat exchangers must be determined manually. The required Dowtherm mass flow rate can be 
determined in the same manner as the flow rates for steam, as demonstrated for the cumene example, 
using equation ( 7 ) and the Hv value for Dowtherm at 350°C. Since Dowtherm A has an atmospheric boiling 
point of 257.1°C, the heating will be done with Dowtherm vapor at 350 °C, at which Hv = 105.3 Btu/lb (31) = 
244.93 kJ/kg. In this manner a total Dowtherm A flow rate of 97416.46 kg/hr was determined, in order to 
satisfy all four heat exchangers. In order to transfer this into USD/hr, a unit price in USD/kg for Dowtherm A 
is required. This can be determined in the same manner that those used for steam, cooling water or other 
==PAGE:83==
75 | P a g e  
 
 
utility unit prices readily available, have been. This requires determining the cost of preparing the required 
flow rate of Dowtherm vapor at 350 °C.  
The Dowtherm will, as is the case with steam and cooling water, be supplied to the different demanding 
units via a recycle loop, which returns the condensed Dowtherm to a boiler for reheating back to vapor 
phase, before once again used to transfer heat to the demanding unit operations. Taking into account, that 
the Dowtherm can be recycled for a period of 5 years before having to be replaced, a purchase of 97416.46 
kg of Dowtherm A would be able to be recycled and satisfy the 4 heat exchangers duty needs for 5 years. 
Assuming a purchase cost of 5 USD/kg (41) an annual cost can be determined to be 
                       
                  
⁄
       
                   
Next the cost of heating the Dowtherm liquid to a 350 °C vapor is determined. The first step is heating the 
Dowtherm liquid from room temperature to its boiling point of 257.1°C. From a Dowtherm A product 
technical data report (31), saturated liquid specific heat capacities for this temperature range, in intervals of 
5°C, are given. Using these, energy requirements for heating 97416.46 kg/hr are determined for each 5 °C 
temperature increase from 25°C to 257.1°C, using equation ( 8 ). The first increase will require:

⁄
                     
All the energy requirements are summed up to a total energy requirement of 4.13·107 kJ/hr for heating the 
Dowtherm to its boiling point. The next energy requirement will be in order for the Dowtherm to change 
from liquid to vapor phase. At 251.7 °C, a Hv value for Dowtherm of 1.275 Btu/lb = 296.57 kJ/kg (31) has been 
obtained, and hence the amount of energy required to vaporize 97416.46 kg/hr of Dowtherm is found to 
be 
                                     
  
⁄
                
Lastly the energy requirement for heating the now Dowtherm vapor the last step to 350°C from 257.1°C, is 
determined. This is done by this time using the Dowtherm A product technical data report given saturated 
vapor specific heat capacities, together with ( 7 ) for this temperature range, in intervals of 5°C and 
summing up the energy requirements to a total of 1.76·107 kJ/hr.  
Summing up, an overall energy requirement in order to prepare Dowtherm A vapor at 350°C is found to be 
8.957·107 kJ/hr = 89.57 GJ/hr. This will be the duty of the boiler requiring the energy supplied from natural

Using these two unit prices, utility costs can be determined manually by multiplying with their heat 
exchanger duties or determined required Dowtherm flow rates. The results are seen in Table 4-7 together 
with the rest of the utility costs associated with steam and cooling water, calculated manually and by ECON. 
Deviations between the energy-based utility costs (those found in ECON) and the mass based manually 
calculated utility costs have also been prepared. Once again it can be seen that for cooling water, 
deviations of approximately 7% have been obtained, while for HP and LP steam deviations of 3.67 and 
26.12% have been calculated.  Since the unit prices for the Dowtherm have been deduced simultaneously, 
the deviations are minor, mainly due to decimal roundups. In the remainder of the analysis the Dowtherm 
energy-based utility costs estimates are those used, together with those determined by ECON for the rest.

Consulting Figure 4-4, it can be seen that the trend that high energy consuming unit operations also will incur 
high utility costs, with the exception of units using cooling water, still holds and that of course the unit 
operations requiring heating are those responsible for the majority of the utility costs. F1 and c-T2 are,

extremely high, due to their relatively high duties due to heating with Dowtherm, which is more than 
double as expensive as HP steam. After these two, the two reboilers r-T1 and r-T2 are the ones ranking 
highest. Based on these results, it can be concluded that E1 and E4 are the process bottlenecks identified in 
this analysis.

again used.

the process is not very profitable with a payback time of 7.74 years and only a 3% rate of return and net 
profit of approximately 4.25 million USD over 10 years.

4.2.5 Step 1.5 – Design Targets 
The results acquired from SustainPro are cross-checked with those of economic evaluation and the LCSoft 
analysis. Table 4-10 summarizes which unit operations were identified as bottlenecks by each of the three 
analyzes. Again, those marked in the SustainPro column corresponds to the units included in the high 
potential EWC paths. In this table it can be seen that the heat exchangers E1 and E4 have been found to be 
global bottlenecks.  
Table 4-10. Summary of main identified bottlenecks by SustainPro, LCSoft and ECON. 
Unit operation 
SustainPro

SustainPro found that the highest EWC values were connected to OP 12, C1, C2 and C8. By addressing the 
energy requirements of E1 and E4, the EWC values of OP 12, C1 and C2 are automatically addressed, seeing 
that these 3 paths all contain the two global bottlenecks. For C8, a reduction in the EWC value would 
require duty reductions for either F1, r-T1, r-T2, c-T3 or C1.  
SustainPro identified a negative MVA value for OP 6 due to the large flow rate of methane in S18 which is 
associated with a low value. An improvement of this indicator would require reducing the flow rate of S18 
which can be obtained by decreasing the separation efficiency of the membrane, allowing more methane 
to be recycled to the process. However since methane is a nonbeneficial byproduct that doesn’t partake in 
any reactions and furthermore also is added continuously to the process via S1, the latter would result in 
more methane accumulating in the process. Thus this material loss is not addressed, as and since OP 6 was 
not connected to a high potential EWC indicator, this path is omitted from here on.  
It is obvious that the main issues identified throughout the full sustainability analysis are: 
 
Utility costs are very high in heat exchangers E1 and E4  
 
Low profit 
 
Carbon footprint is high for F1 
==PAGE:89==
81 | P a g e  
 
 
 
Carbon footprint is also high for E1 and E4 
Accordingly design target specifications in order to overcome these issues are: 
 
Decrease utility costs for E1 and E4 
 
Increase profitability of process 
 
Decrease carbon footprint of F1 
 
Decrease carbon footprint of E1 and E4 
There are a few possible ways of decreasing the utility costs of E1 and E4. One way would be to split the 
heating duties of each heat exchanger into two, by adding an extra heat exchanger prior to each, in which 
each process stream is preheated as much as possible by the means of HP steam. This would allow part of 
the energy requirement to be satisfied by HP steam, which is much cheaper than Dowtherm, reducing the 
Dowtherm requirement from E1 and E4 and hence their utility costs. Another way is by using the same 
approach of splitting the heating duty of each heat exchanger into two, but instead of supplying the first 
heat exchanger with HP steam, the heat contained in the effluent from the reactor, which comes out at a 
temperature of approximately 606 K, is exploited. By using the reactor effluent as heat transfer medium in 
the two preheating heat exchangers, a simultaneous cooling of the effluent is obtained before fed to the 
flash, also reducing the cooling duty required by the flash. This solution would reduce the Dowtherm 
requirements without any additional costs, improving the profitability of the process. Simultaneously it will 
also address the aspect of the last two target specification requiring lower carbon footprints for E1, E4 and 
F1. 
Besides the profitability improvement that can be achieved by incorporating the above given proposal, the 
profitability of the process can also be increased, if the reboiler duties of r-T1 and r-T2 could be lowered. 
Therefore by assessing the design of T1 and T2, further utility costs reductions could result. 
Based on the above, it is obvious that the alteration proposed to reduce the energy demands of E1 and E4 
also will improve the other design targets, and therefore appears to be a highly beneficial solution. In the 
following sections better and more sustainable alternative designs including the above mentioned 
alteration are proposed.  
 
==PAGE:90==
82 | P a g e  
 
 
4.3 Part 2 – Generation and screening of new design alternatives 
New design alternatives are now proposed for the HDA process in order to meet the design targets 
specifications set in the previous section, and establish a more sustainable solution. 
4.3.1 Step 2.1 – Generation of new design alternatives 
4.3.1.1 New design alternative 1 
This main focus of this first design alternative was to reduce the utility costs of E1 and E4. However first a 
minor alteration is merged into this design alternative. It was investigated whether changing compressor C1 
to only do the task of increasing the pressure to 40 atm would decrease its duty. Therefore the second 
specification regarding the output temperature is altered to a 100% adiabatic efficiency specification. A 
heat exchanger is then inserted after C1 with the purpose of performing the task of heating the stream to 
600 K. Here it was found that this solution reduced the duty of C1 significantly, and the new heat exchanger 
was only associated with a low duty. Even though no design target specifications requiring a reduction in 
the utility costs of C1 were established, this alteration is expected to be slightly profitable and is therefore 
kept.  
The mentioned solution is now incorporated by inserting three new heat exchangers (E6, E7 and E8), prior 
to E4, E3 and E1 respectively. E3 is included in order to achieve additional utility costs. The integration of 
the reactor effluent in these preheaters can be done through a few different setups. A ‘parallel’ setup in 
which the reactor output stream (S4) is split up into 3 streams, one to supply each heat exchanger, 
requiring the addition of a splitter as well as a mixer in order to mix the three streams together again was 
investigated. This allows all three heat exchangers to be supplied with a heating stream at the same 
temperature. Another alternative investigated was to use an ‘in series’ type of setup, in which S4 is passed 
through all three heat exchangers, one after the other, gradually reducing the temperature of the reactor 
effluent and hence supplying the three heat exchangers with decreasing heating utility temperatures. For 
this case the order of supply may be essential, in the sense that it would be beneficial to supply the heat 
exchangers processing lower flow rates first, so the gradual decrease is slowed down, allowing a higher 
utility stream input temperature when reaching the final heat exchanger. The solution which resulted in an 
overall lowest duty of E1, E3 and E4, was by using the ‘in series’ setup. In all three preheaters the heat 
exchangers are set to heat the process stream as much as possible, still allowing decent temperature 
differences between the two streams, and not calling for extremely large heat exchanger areas. This gave 
cold product temperatures of 530 K, 480 K and 440 K exiting from E6, E7 and E8 respectively.  
==PAGE:91==
83 | P a g e  
 
 
The alternative, using HP steam in E6, E7 and E8 was also investigated. This allowed cold product 
temperatures of 500 K from all three, and overall resulted in a lower Dowtherm requirement by E1, E3 and 
E4. However, the difference was not high enough to allow the additional cost of the HP steam to be taken 
into account and still be less costly compared to the solution of using the reactor effluent as heating utility, 
which does not incur any additional utility cost. This idea is therefore disregarded again. 
The resultant new design alternative flow sheet is seen in Figure 4-6. 
4.3.1.2 New design alternative 2 
This second design alternative is set up in order to investigate whether the capital investment of the 
process could be decreased by replacing the 8 heat exchangers from design alternative by two larger heat 
exchangers, and improving the profitability of the process in this manner. Therefore all the heat exchangers 
prior to the mixer M2 are eliminated and replaced by two heat exchangers after the mixer. The first is 
supplied with heat from the reactor effluent, which is sent to the flash immediately after, while the second 
heat exchanger is supplied with Dowtherm A. The resultant new design alternative flow sheet is seen in 
Figure 4-7. 
4.3.1.3 New design alternative 3 
The base case design reactor was modeled as an isothermal reactor, cooled with cooling water. However, 
since the reactor effluent can be used as heating medium to preheat the incoming process streams, the 
higher the temperature of the reactor effluent, the better. Hence it would be silly not to exploit the 
exothermability of the reactions by using an adiabatic reactor instead. This allows a temperature increase 
to take place in the reactor, and hence more heat will be available in the reactor effluent for transfer in E6, 
E7 and E8. This design alternative thus only includes the alteration of the reactor to an adiabatic reactor, 
and the flowsheet is identical to that seen in Figure 4-7. 
4.3.1.4 Other attempted alterations 
In order to further increase the overall profit, the aspect of lowering the column reboiler duties was also 
addressed. Using short cut columns in PRO II, minimum reflux ratio and tray calculations were performed 
for both columns T1 and T2 in order to investigate the possibility of whether an increase in the amount of 
trays could lead to lower reboiler duties. Here the results showed that only minimal reboiler duty 
reductions were associated with a significant increase in the number of trays and thus reduced reboiler 
duties have not been achieved.  
==PAGE:92==
84 | P a g e

==PAGE:93==

4.3.2 Step 2.2 – New design alternatives rigorous simulation 
The new design alternatives were simulated in PRO II. For the newly added heat exchanger E5, the same 
overall heat transfer coefficient as used for E4 (U = 0.085 kW/m2·°C) has been specified, since the process 
stream heated in this heat exchanger is mainly toluene. For E7 and E8, overall heat transfer coefficients of 
U = 0.15 kW/m2·°C (42) for gases at high pressure inside and outside the tubes, have been assumed. For E6 a 
value of U = 0.20 kW/m2·°C (42) for high pressure gas on one side, versus liquid on the other, was assumed. 
For the two new larger heat exchangers E1 and E2 in ND 2, the latter value of 0.15 kW/m2·°C was specified 
for E1, while for E2 the same value (U = 0.11 kW/m2·°C) assumed previously was specified.   
The obtained simulation mass and energy balance results are given in Appendix Table B-7 through Table B-8. 
Note that the mass balance results for ND 3 and ND 4 are the same, as only the temperatures of some 
streams, and hence the energy balance, vary between these two alternatives. Therefore the mass and 
energy balance data for ND 3 and ND 4 are gathered in Table B-8. From these tables it can be seen, that the 
required reaction ratio has been complied in all alternatives. The calculated duties and PRO II estimated 
sizing parameters for each design alternative are given in Table 4-11. Table 4-13 summarizes the overall energy 
demands.

In order to justify the changes incorporated into all the alternatives, a few simple economic calculations are 
performed. For the first minor alteration, adding E5 resulted in C1’s duty reducing from 1.24 GJ/hr to 0.41 
GJ/hr, corresponding to a savings of 0.1 million USD/year. The duty of E5 was found to be 0.23, which using 
a unit price of 23.09 USD/GJ for heating with Dowtherm A, results in a utility cost of around 38000 
USD/years. Summing up the new utility costs of C1 and E5, an overall savings of about 62000 USD/year is 
obtained. The extra capital investment required for such an extra heat exchanger was estimated in ECON to 
be around 8312 USD, which compared to the size of the savings, is paid off quickly.  
 Secondly, looking at the duties of heat exchangers E1, E3 and E4, their duties in the base case summed up 
to 23.5 GJ/hr that required heating by Dowtherm. After addition of E6, E7 and E8, the duties of E1, E3 and 
E4, summed up have reduced to 10.06 GJ/hr corresponding to a 2.23 million USD/year savings. In addition 
to this the flash’s duty has also been reduced from 27.26 GJ/hr to 13.75 GJ/hr which further increases the 
annual savings slightly by about 34000 USD/year. The extra capital investment connected to the addition of 
the three extra heat exchangers were based on the determined areas from Table 4-11, found to be 79284 
USD. Again compared to the size of the overall savings, this extra capital investment is paid off fast.  
Looking at ND 2, it can be seen in Table 4-11, that the Dowtherm requiring duty of E1 through E5 sums up to 
10.63 GJ/hr, while the duty of the bigger new E2 has a duty of 10.56 GJ/hr, being only slightly lower and 
yielding only a savings of around 11000 USD/year. However when cost estimated, the two new heat 
exchangers E1 and E2 in ND 2, a total capital investment of 98518 USD results, which is 68285 USD lower 
than the capital investment of all smaller heat exchangers in ND 2. Thus the capital investment and 
Dowtherm utility cost has been reduced slightly by employing two larger heat exchangers in place of 8 
smaller ones, and this alteration is therefore found to be profitable, even though the extent is not that 
significant.  
Looking at ND 3, it can be seen that the energy demand of the new E2 has reduced to 4.44 from 10.56 
GJ/hr, which yields a savings of about 1 million USD/year. In addition to this, the energy demand of the 
reactor has been eradicated from its previous value of 5.2 GJ/hr, corresponding to an additional small 
saving of 13000 USD/year. However the higher temperature of S4, which from Table B-8 can be seen now is 
==PAGE:96==
88 | P a g e  
 
 
718.25 K, a higher input temperature of the stream to the flash, compared to ND 2 has been obtained, and 
hence increasing the duty of the flash slightly again to 18.00 GJ/hr. This is however only adds an additional 
minor cost of around 10000 USD/year, and hence an additional overall savings of close to 1 million 
USD/year has been obtained with the last alteration.

Comparing with the base case (Table 4-8), it can be seen that the total purchase cost has decreased by 75699 
USD in going from the base case to ND 1, despite the fact that 4 extra heat exchangers have been added. 
This is due to the reduced duty of C1 resulting in the purchase cost of C1 reducing from 253450 USD to 
==PAGE:98==
90 | P a g e  
 
 
119739 USD. In addition to this, the areas of E1 through E4 and hence their capital cost estimates have also 
reduced, due to their reduced duties. Since these two reductions exceed the extra costs introduced by the 
three new heat exchangers, an overall purchase cost and consequently TCI reduction is obtained. 
Comparing the annual energy consumption, utility costs and operating costs, it can be seen that an 
significant decrease in all three have been achieved from the base case to ND 1. This is of course mainly 
due to the lowered energy requirements of E1, E3, E4, C1 and F1. Consequently the process has become 
more profitable, with an impressive net profit increase from 4.25 million USD to 23.74 million USD, and a 
payback time reduction to 3.42 years. 
Comparing the results of ND 1 to those of ND 2 it can be seen that for the case of the purchase cost and 
hence the TCI, a reduction has been achieved, due to as mentioned, the two larger heat exchangers being 
cheaper than the 8 smaller ones. The utility cost is also slightly lower for ND 2, as a result of the slightly 
reduced Dowtherm requirement, which only lead to minor profitability improvements.  
Taking the results of ND 3 into account, it can be seen that in all aspects, and improvement has been made. 
The capital cost however has increased slightly, and this is mainly due to the new reactor conditions, which 
has resulted in a larger reactor. With this design alternative, the net profit has increased further by 
approximately 8 million USD, rendering ND 3 as the most profitable alternative.
