4.4 Part  3 – Comparison of base case design against new design alternatives 
The analysis results obtained from the full sustainability analysis of the new design alternatives are now 
compared in more detail.  
4.4.1 Step 3.1 – Comparison of  full sustainability results 
The duty requirements of the base case from Table 4-2 are depicted together with those of the new design 
alternatives from Table 4-11. This visual comparison is seen in Figure 4-8.  
The alterations incorporated did not have any effect on C2, nor the columns, and hence it can be seen that 
for all designs the energy requirement of the latter roughly have stayed constant. For the reactor, it can be 
seen that the duties are constant, until ND 3, where its duty has been eliminated.  
For F1, it can be seen that in going from the base case to ND 1, the duty has decreased significantly from 
27.86 to 13.75 GJ/hr. This is of course due to the input stream to the flash having a lower temperature than 
in the base case, due to the cooling of the reactor effluent through E6, E7 and E8. The merging of the 
smaller heat exchangers into the two larger heat exchangers and heating the first with the reactor effluent 
stream, resulted the reactor effluent being cooled slightly further to 416.44 K before entering the flash, 
resulting in the minimal additional reduction that can be seen for ND 2. However the alteration of the 
reactor from isothermal to adiabatic in ND 3 has resulted in a slight increase of the latter temperature 
again, slightly increasing the duty of F1 again in this alternative. 
For C1 it can be seen that in going from the base case design to the first new design alternative, the energy 
requirement has been reduced from 1.24 to 0.41 GJ/hr as a result of the addition of E5, which 
simultaneously only adds an energy requirement of 0.23 GJ/hr for this heat exchanger. Hereafter the duty 
stays constant, as the following alterations do not affect C1 further.  
Finally regarding the heat exchangers, Figure 4-8 visually shows the extent of the reductions achieved for E1, 
E3 and E4 by the addition of E6, E7 and E8 prior to these. These reductions are equivalent to the duties 
emerged for E6, E7 and E8, however these duties are satisfied by heat transferred from the rector effluent, 
and hence are not connected to any additional expenses. It is also apparent that for ND 2, no duties are 
visual for the heat exchangers E1 through E8, since these have been replaced by the two new larger ones.

For ND 1, the duties of E6, E7 and E8 sum up to 13.21 GJ/hr, while the Dowtherm requiring heat exchangers 
E1 through E5 required a total of 10.63 GJ/hr. In ND 2 the new E1, which has substituted E6 through E8, has 
a duty of 13.52 GJ/hr which is slightly higher than the summed duties of E6 through E8 in ND 1. Hence the 
merging of these heat exchangers has allowed an overall larger preheating, allowing a slightly lower 
Dowtherm requirement in the new larger E2. This is reasoned by the fact that for the new ND 2 E1, the 
entire energy demand is supplied with a reactor effluent with a temperature of 600 K, in contrast to for the 
smaller exchangers, for which the temperature of the effluent decreased from heat exchanger to heat 
exchanger.  
Furthermore, it can be seen that the significant reduction in the new E2 in going from ND 2 to ND3, is 
equivalent to the energy demand increase for the new E1, since a larger preheating has been achieved with 
the increased reactor effluent temperature.   
Overall the energy requirement of the process decreases from 79.74 GJ/hr to 51.45 GJ/hr from the base 
case to ND 1, while for ND 2 an overall energy consumption of 51.46 GJ/hr has been obtained. With the 
introduction of ND 3, this value is further decreased to 44.42 GJ/hr.   
Next the SustainPro results are addressed. Comparing the paths for the new design alternatives given in 
Table 4-13 to those of the base case in Table 4-5, it can be seen that the base case identified open paths are 
comparable to those of ND 1. The only difference is that reactor output bypasses the integrated heat 
exchangers on its way to the flash. The addition of the integrated units to the different paths also won’t 
add extra energy costs to the paths. The comparison between ND 1 and ND 2 is difficult, while a direct 
comparison can be made between ND 2 and ND 3. The comparison of the indicators of the equivalent paths 
is seen in Table 4-17.

obtained F1 duty reduction. For C1 and C2, the EWC values have also improved, due to the large reduction 
of E1’s energy requirement. For C8, it can be seen that the EWC value has also been reduced, due to the

alternative, which have already been discussed. Most importantly it should be noticed that the significant 
utility costs reductions of the two global bottlenecks E1 and E4, which in ND 2 and ND 3 are replaced by 
new E2, have been achieved. Compared to this, the cooling utility cost reduction achieved for F1, is minor, 
despite the large energy demand decrease.

case to ND 3, an overall Dowtherm utility cost saving of 81.4% has been achieved.

4.4.2 Step 3.2 – Selection of best alternative 
The detailed comparison of a wide range of sustainability metrics are summarized in Table 4-19. The 
deviation given is between the base case design and ND 3. Here it should be remembered, that a positive 
deviation represents a decrease in the variable value in going from the base case to the new design, which 
in most cases is equal to an improvement. It can be seen that all deviations, with the exception of the 
deviations for the rate of return and the net profit, which are negative. However these are two of the 
situations where the opposite holds, and an increase is equal to a positive outcome.  
A percentage comparison of the most important sustainability metrics regarding the target specifications is 
seen in Table 4-20. The corresponding radar plot is given in Figure 4-11.  
For all three design alternatives, is can be seen that large improvements with respect to the design targets, 
have been obtained in all three design alternatives with the exception of the operating cost, where only 
small improvements have been made. The design target specifications regarding the global bottle necks 
have been improved significantly, leading to a better economy of the process and consequently a much 
higher profitability, with a 651% improvement of the net profit achieved by ND 3. 
From the radar plot it can be seen that all three solutions are non-tradeoff solutions, as none of them lie 
outside the boundary given by the base case. In addition to this, it is obvious, that ND 1 and ND 2 have 
achieved similar results, with ND 2 being only slightly better, mainly with respect to profitability. It is also 
apparent that ND 3 exceeds both ND 1 and ND 2 in almost all aspects, apart from the carbon footprint of 
F1, and hence ND 3 is found to be the most sustainable alternative that best meets the design target 
specifications. Overall ND 3 is also a far more sustainable solution than the base case.  
Based on the above, it has been emphasized how a decrease in expensive heating utility consumptions can 
improve the overall profit of the process meaningfully, stressing the importance of assessing the 
sustainability of especially unit operations associated with heating duties.

5 Case study 2: Bioethanol process 
Both the conceptual example and HDA case study include relatively small and simple process flowsheets 
that are easy to keep track of. Next the computer-aided framework is applied to a more advanced and 
larger flowsheet. This time the chosen base case is a bioethanol process flowsheet established in 
conjunction with a PhD thesis by Merlin Alvarado-Morales (43). The process is based on the main production 
areas of a bioethanol process documented by NREL (National Renewable Energy Laboratory) by Wooley et 
al. (44), which involves the production of ethanol from lignocellulosic biomass, through dilute acid 
prehydrolysis, enzymatic hydrolysis and fermentation. In his thesis, Morales already examines the NREL 
bioethanol process for possible improvements. In this regard he investigates the possibility of using solvent-
based extractive distillation for separation of the water-ethanol azeotrope. He examines the performance 
of various possible solvent candidates, including organic solvents, ionic liquids and hyperbranched 
polymers. Based on this a bioethanol production process flowsheet with an altered downstream 
purification process area has been established. The original NREL downstream purification area included 
two distillation columns for pre-concentrating the ethanol to near azeotropic composition, followed by 
vapor phase molecular sieve adsorption to break the azeotrope and remove the residual water for nearly 
pure ethanol with a purity of approximately 99.5 % (43). The new process flowsheet utilizes a downstream 
process area, in which the second distillation column and molecular sieve adsorption unit have been 
replaced by a extractive distillation column using the organic solvent ethylene glycol as entrainer, followed 
by a recovery distillation column for retrieval of the solvent for recycle. The developed flowsheet has 
already been set up and simulated in PRO II, and the PRO II flowsheet is acquired and used as the base case 
design for this case study.   
5.1 Base case design 
The base case flowsheet only includes the main areas of the bioethanol process, once again excluding the 
areas concerning feedstock handling, utility preparation or any product processing areas, etc. The process 
involves production of bioethanol from lignocellulosic biomass, in the form of hardwood chips with the 
composition as given below in Table 5-1.  
Table 5-1. Feedstock composition. The acetate content of the acetate present in the hemicellulose polymer. 
Component 
%Dry basis (mass) 
Cellulose 
43.1 
Hemicellulose 
19.24 
Arabinan 
0.80 
Mannan 
3.97 
Galactan 
0.24 
Lignin 
27.96 
Acetate 
4.69 
Water 
48.15 
==PAGE:110==
102 | P a g e  
 
 
 
The first area of the process is the pre-treatment area, in which the feedstock is pretreated. In this area the 
biomass is treated with dilute sulfuric acid at high temperature for a short time in a pre-treatment reactor, 
liberating the hemicellulose. The woodchips are first heated to about 100 °C by adding low pressure steam. 
Subsequently diluted sulfuric acid is added and the mixture is heated again by adding high pressure steam. 
The mixture is then added to a pre-treatment reactor (R1), operated at 190 °C and 12.2 atm. The residence 
time in this reactor is 10 minutes (44).  
In this reactor, most of the hemicellulose in the feedstock is converted to xylose by hydrolysis reactions. 
The arabinan, mannan and galactan contents are converted to arabinose, mannose, and galactose 
respectively, and a small portion of the cellulose is converted to glucose. In addition to this, the acetate 
contained in the hemicellulose polymer is converted to acetic acid. The pre-treatment reactor conditions 
also lead to solubilization of the lignin in the feedstock and exposes the cellulose for subsequent enzymatic 
hydrolysis. In addition to this, degradation products of the pentose sugars and hexose sugars, primarily 
furfural and hydroxymethylfurfural (HMF) respectively, are also formed. Following the pre-treatment 
reactor, the hydrolysate slurry is flash cooled to 1 atm (F1), vaporizing and removing a large amount of the 
water and a portion of the acetic acid, furfural and HMF. The residence time is this flash has been given to 
be 10 minutes (44). The removal of these heterocyclic aldehydes is important, as these components are toxic 
to the microorganisms in the fermentation. The reactions that take place in the pretreatment section are 
summarized below in Table 5-2. The modeled conversions are also given in this table. 
Table 5-2. Pretreatment Hydrolyzer reactions and conversions. These reactions are modeled as occurring simultaneously. 
 
Reaction 
Conversion 
 
              →

Following the pre-treatment area is the detoxification area, which includes ion exchange and overliming for 
further removal of the compounds that will be toxic to the fermenting organism. These will only be present 
in the liquid hydrolysate and therefore, first the unreacted solids are separated from the liquid hydrolysate 
in a solid/liquid separator, assumed to be a filter press (SC1). After filtering, the filtrate is cooled to the ion 
==PAGE:111==
103 | P a g e  
 
 
 
exchange operating temperature of 40°C in a heat exchanger (E1). Ion exchange (R2) is used to remove the 
acetic acid and sulfuric acid, which are toxic to the microorganisms in the following fermentation. This is 
achieved by letting the latter react with ammonia. It is assumed that approximately 88% of the acetic acid 
and 100% of the sulfuric acid is removed (44).  
After the ion exchange, a portion of water is removed (SC2), before the liquid hydrolysate is overlimed. This 
is done by first reacidifying the liquid hydrolysate to pH 2 by addition of sulfuric acid and then adding lime 
(calcium hydroxide) to raise the pH to 10 and heating by direct injection of LP steam to 50 °C. The liquid 
hydrolysate is then held at these conditions for 1 hour in an overliming tank (R3). The liquid hydrolysate is 
then adjusted to the fermentation pH of 4.5 in a reacidification tank (R4) and held for 4 hours. The latter 
allows precipitation of gypsum (calcium sulfate). The gypsum is then removed in a rotary drum filter (SC3), 
which is assumed to completely remove all of the gypsum. After the removal of the gypsum, the 
conditioned hydrolysate is mixed with the previously removed solid fraction, in a slurry tank (M12), for 
which a residence time of 15 minutes has been stated (44). The slurry is now ready for saccharification and 
fermentation in the SSCF (Simultaneous saccharification and co-fermentation) section. The reactions that 
take place in the ion exchanger, overliming tank and reacidification tank, and their corresponding 
conversions, are summarized below in Table 5-3. 
Table 5-3. Reactions taking place in the ion exchange, overliming and conditioning process. The two first reactions take place in 
the IX, while the third is modeled to occur in the overliming tank and the reacidification tank (44).  
 
Ion exchange 
 
Reaction

Calcium hydroxide 1.00 
 
A small portion of the detoxified slurry is sent to the SSCF seed process area for production of Zymomonas 
mobilis (ZM) microorganisms. The saccharification (hydrolysis) of the remaining cellulose into glucose 
begins to take place, using purchased cellulase enzymes which are added together with nutrients in the 
form of corn steep liquor (CLS). The glucose and supplied CLS is then utilized by the ZM microorganisms to 
reproduce as well as ferment the glucose and previously formed xylose into ethanol, as well as side 
products in the form of larger amounts of CO2 and smaller amounts of glycerol, xylitol, succinic acid, lactic 
acid and acetic acid. The SSCF seed process area will include 2 fermenter trains including 5 seed fermenters 
each (44), and the seed fermenters will each operate at 30 °C. The incoming hydrolysate is at approximately 
55 °C and hence is first cooled in a heat exchanger (E2) prior to inlet. The effluent from the seed SSCF 
==PAGE:112==
104 | P a g e  
 
 
 
fermenters is sent to a seed holding tank (SC5), from which it is used to inoculate the bulk of the detoxified 
slurry, before it is sent to the main production SSCF train of continuous fermenters, for further 
simultaneous saccharification and co-fermentation. A total of 17 production fermenters, arranged in three 
continuous trains, are required in the SSCF production process area with a total residence time of 7 days (44).  
The bulk detoxified hydrolysate slurry is prior to mixing with the seed inoculum cooled to 35°C (E3). Larger 
quantities of CLS and cellulase are added once more and the mixture is fed to the SSCF production 
fermenter trains.  The resulting ethanol broth is collected and sent to a flash (F2) in order to remove a large 
amount of the produced CO2 before separating the remaining solids from the broth by centrifugation (SO1) 
and then further filtration (SC4) to remove any trace solid residues, before proceeding to the product 
recovery process area. The saccharification and fermentation reactions that take place in the SSCF area are 
summarized below in Table 5-4.The fermentation reactions are the same for both the seed and production 
fermenters, with varying conversions. It should also be noted, that the ammonia is shown as a reactant for 
material balance purposes only. Corn steep liquor is the actual nitrogen source. Besides the production of 
the side products mentioned earlier, additional loss to side products also occur due to contaminating 
organisms. The modeled contamination loss reactions are given in Table 5-5.

The product recovery area involves three distillation columns, as it is not possible to obtain pure ethanol 
directly in one-step by ordinary distillation, due to the presence of the water/ethanol azeotrope. The first 
column T1 (beer column) removes the dissolved CO2 and most of the water with the effluent, removing as 
little ethanol as possible with this stream. More than 90% of the water is removed with the effluent. This 
column operates at 1 atm and the inlet which is fed to stage 17 is heated to 92.5°C in a heat exchanger (E4) 
prior to the column.  
The ethanol rich distillate from the beer column is fed directly to the next column, at tray 22 at a 
temperature of approximately 79°C. This column (T2) is an extractive distillation column operated at 1 atm, 
which uses ethylene glycol as solvent. The ethylene glycol is fed to the column at stage 3, and at a 
temperature of approximately 75°C. This allows the separation of a 99.86% pure ethanol distillate product 
stream. The solvent is recovered in the bottoms stream together with the residual water.  
The effluent from T2 is cooled to around 107°C (E5), which at 1 atm is a suitable temperature for separation 
of the water from the solvent in the third column, the recovery column (T3). The stream is fed to stage 5 of 
this column. The recovery column recovers the solvent with the effluent, and this stream leaves the 
recovery column at 197°C. The recovered solvent is therefore cooled to 75°C (E6) before mixing with the 
make-up ethylene glycol and sent for reuse in the extractive column. In this way the overall ethylene glycol 
requirement is at a minimum, equal to the amount lost with the ethanol product stream and the third 
column distillate. A controller is present, adjusting the ethylene glycol feed according to the composition of 
the ethanol rich feed stream to the extractive distillation column, in order to ensure the correct ratio in 
order to continuously obtain an ethanol product at 99.86% purity.  
The beer column consists of 32 theoretical steps, while the extractive column includes 30 theoretical steps, 
and the recovery column 15 steps.  
The flowsheet is divided into three. The pretreatment, ion exchange and overliming process area flowsheet 
is seen in Figure 5-1. The SSCF section, and product purification section is seen in Figure 5-2 and Figure 5-3 
respectively. 
==PAGE:114==
106 | P a g e  
 
 
 
From the flowsheets it can be seen, that the SSCF seed fermenters have been modeled as one large seed 
fermenter R5, while the production fermenters have been modeled into R6. The loss to side reactions is 
modeled as a side stream bypassing R6, through another reactor R7, and hence R7 is not an actual reactor 
that needs to be taken into account in the economic analysis. It is merely only present for the modeling 
aspect of the contamination loss. Hence SC7 is also not to be taken into account. Based on this, the reactors 
R6 and R7 are accounted for as one, overall giving the energy requirement and utility cost associated with 
the in reality, multiple SSCF production fermenters.

5.2 Part 1 – Process simulation, sustainability analysis and bottleneck 
identification 
The framework is now applied to the above given bioethanol process flowsheet in order to identify process 
bottlenecks. 
5.2.1 Step 1.1 –Base case rigorous simulation 
The wood chip feedstock is supplied at a rate of 159115.7 kg/hr. Water is supplied to the process through 
S5, S15 and S22, as well as together with the cellulase input streams S54 and S73. All together this gives a 
total fresh water input of 305962 kg/hr. The water added via S15 is meant to account for the water that is 
used to rinse the solid fraction separated in in the filter press. Sulfuric acid is added through S4, S31, S43 at 
a total amount of 1866.15 kg/hr. LP steam (164 °C) and HP steam (268°) are fed to the process at 19459 
kg/hr and 44596 kg/hr respectively. Lime is added through S34 at 714 kg/hr, while a total of 696 kg 
cellulase/hr is required. The ethylene glycol feed rate through S84 amounts to 405.2 kg/hr. For the case of 
ammonia, the in theory only input to the process is the 683.52 kg/hr that is fed through S21. Streams S48 
and S74 are not actual ammonia inputs, and have only been added for modeling aspects, as the amount 
equivalent to the nitrogen available in the added CLS. Therefore in the modeled flowsheet, CLS acts as an 
inert that does not get consumed.  
In order to take the reactions that take place in the ion exchange, overliming tank and reacidification tank 
into account, these have been modeled as reactors in the flowsheet, and all reactors were modeled as 
isothermal conversion reactors, that will require either heating or cooling in order to maintain the correct 
temperatures, dependent on whether the reactions involved are exothermic or endothermic.  
All heat exchangers are modeled as countercurrent heat exchangers and the three columns are all 
equipped with both a column condensers and reboiler. The filtration units have been modeled as stream 
calculators, yielding expected filtration separation efficiencies. All the pumps included only are associated 
will minor pressure increases, as the purpose of the pumps are to transfer the streams from one unit to 
another.  
With respect to the type of compounds found in the bioethanol production process, in the PRO II model, 
we distinguish between two main classes of compounds: Compounds that are involved in vapor-liquid 
equilibria, and compounds that are only present as insoluble solids and therefore their effect on vapor-
liquid equilibria may be ignored. The compounds that are only present as insoluble solids are ammonium 
==PAGE:119==
111 | P a g e  
 
 
 
sulfate, ammonium acetate, cellulose, hemicellulose, arabinan, mannan, galactan, cellulase, ZM, lignin, 
CaSO4 (gypsum), CaH2O2 (lime) and acetate.  
Many of the compounds, such as water, ethanol, acetic acid, sulfuric acids and so on, are already included 
in PRO II’s database. However compounds such as glucose, xylose, cellulose, lignin, ZM, cellulase and so on, 
are not a part of the PRO II library and must be defined manually. This task his accomplished by loading an 
already prepared library file to PRO II before running the simulation file.  
The simulation mass and energy balance results acquired from PRO II are comprehensive and have not 
been given in the Appendix. Instead these can be consulted on the attached CD-ROM.  From here it can be 
seen, that an ethanol product stream of 99.86% purity at a rate of 19730.61 kg/hr has been obtained. The 
PRO II calculated unit sizing parameters and duties are seen below in Table 5-6. The duties have been 
visualized in Figure 5-4.

==PAGE:120==

From Figure 5-4 it is obvious that c-T1, E4 and r-T1 are the three highest energy requiring units, by far 
exceeding the rest of the units. The reboiler r-T1 slightly surpasses c-T1 and E4 and has the highest energy 
requirement at 101.82 GJ/hr. The reactions taking place in R1, R2 and R3 overall give an endothermic heat 
of reaction and hence require heat input in order to maintain the isothermality of these three units. The 
opposite holds for R4, R5 and R6+R7, for which the reactions that take place in these, overall are 
exothermic, and require cooling in order maintain temperature. All the heat exchangers but E4, perform 
cooling as already mentioned, and the input streams to the flash are also hotter than the flash 
temperature, and hence a flash cooling requiring cooling water is the case in both flash units. All three 
column condensers can sufficiently be cooled with cooling water, while the reboilers will require steam. 
Based on the output process stream temperatures, r-T1 can be satisfied with LP steam, r-T2 will require MP 
steam and r-T3 will require HP steam. 
 Overall the base case has an energy demand of 507.05 GJ/hr (=2.57E-02 GJ/kg product). 234.91 GJ/hr of 
this amount is required for cooling, while 271.86 GJ/hr is required for heating tasks. Only a minor 0.28 
GJ/hr is required for electrical work.  
The areas estimated for E1, E2, E3, E5 and E6 are based on the assumption that cooling water is the utility 
with inlet and return temperatures of t1 = 20°C and t2 = 30°C. For E1, E2 and E3 a U value of 200 
Btu/ft2·hr·ºF (18) (=1.1357 kW/m2·°C) has been assumed, for both the hot and cold fluid being water, since 
the process streams being cooled in these heat exchangers mainly consist of water. For E5 and E6 a value of 
U = 100 Btu/ft2·hr·ºF (18) (=0.567 kW/m2·°C) has been assumed, which holds for an organic solvent being the 
hot fluid, which is the case, since the process streams cooled in these heat exchangers mainly consists of 
ethylene glycol. Oppositely the other heat exchangers, E4 is to heat the process stream to 92.85 °C, and will 
therefore be supplied with LP steam. Hence the area for this heat exchanger has been estimated based on 
the assumption that the hot fluid temperatures are T1 = T2 = 164°C. Since S65 is mainly water, an overall 
heat transfer coefficient of U = 250 Btu/ft2·hr·ºF (18), (=1.4196 kW/m2·°C) for steam as the hot fluid and 
water as the cold fluid, can be assumed.  
From PRO II it can be found, that the overall heat of reaction associated with R1 is -303.96 GJ/hr, while it 
for R2 is -3.80 GJ/hr, for R3 is -1.34 GJ/hr, for R4 is -0.064 GJ/hr, and for R5 is -0.04 GJ/hr. For R6 and R7 
overall heat of reactions of -20.12 and -0.84 GJ/hr have been found. Based on this, it would be expected 
that the duties of the reactors all should be cooling duties in order to remove the heat added by the 
reactions occurring. However from Table 5-6 it can be seen that the duties of R1, R2 and R3 are heating 
duties, while the duties of R4, R5, R6 and R7 are cooling duties. This is due to inlet stream temperatures not 
==PAGE:121==
113 | P a g e  
 
 
 
all being at the fixed reaction temperature specified for the reactors. For example, for the pretreatment 
reactor, the input stream is around 100°C, while the pretreatment reaction temperature is 190°C. 
Therefore heat is required supplied to the reactor to heat the input stream, but due to the exothermal 
nature of the reactions, heat is also supplied in this manner and hence the overall duty estimated for the 
reactor is reduced due to the heat acquired by the reactions.  
5.2.2 Step 1.2 – Mass and energy indicators analysis 
In this case study, the annual operation time is 8406 hours (43).  However, before conducting the mass and 
energy indicators analysis in SustainPro, it is necessary to alter the flowsheet slightly. This is due to the fact, 
that only one inlet stream is allowed to distillation columns in order for SustainPro to determine the open 
and closed paths. Since the extractive distillation column T2 has to stream inputs, it is necessary to add a 
mixer to mix these two input streams, and model the column as a stream calculator with recoveries 
equivalent to those obtained by simulating the column. The flowsheet alteration is illustrated below in 
Figure 5-5.  
 
Figure 5-5. Altered purification process area in order to ensure compatibility with SustainPro. 
 
All the input data specified to SustainPro can be seen in the SustainPro macro spreadsheet, which can be 
found on the CD-ROM. This includes the assumed selling and purchase prices used for the compounds, as 
taken from Morales (43). These are given below in Table 5-7. For the case of the feedstock, the unit price of 
0.03 USD/kg has been specified for each constituent in the feedstock, except for the water content, which 
==PAGE:122==
114 | P a g e  
 
 
 
will be taken into account at a price of 0.0003 USD/kg. In addition to this is should also be noted, that the 
LP steam and MP steam, will be taken into account at the latter price as well, since the constituents of 
these input streams are water. This will be far lower than the actual input value of the steam, which 
according to Table 2-6 will cost around 0.012 USD/kg and 0.016 USD/kg to prepare, for LP and HP steam 
respectively. Therefore these three steam inputs probably won’t get associated with high MVA or TVA 
values by SustainPro.

The resultant complete ordered lists of indictors are due to the extent of the process flowsheet, too 
comprehensive to represent here or in the appendix. Like the input data specified, all the SustainPro results 
including the determined open and closed paths and the complete list of ordered indicators, can be seen in 
the corresponding SustainPro spreadsheet found on the CD-ROM.  
From the SustainPro results it can be seen, that 379 mass open paths and 34 mass cycle paths have been 
found. From the ordered list of indicators it is apparent that 6 open path MVA’s have been given very high 
potential. These same paths have also attained a very high potential TVA value alongside 7 additional path 
TVA’s. With respect to EWC, a total of 24 mass open paths have been assigned with a very high potential. 
With respect to the cycle paths, only one (C23) has been given a very high potential with respect to both its 
EWC and AF values. 
Below in Table 5-8  the values of the different sustainability metrics in the respective paths are 
summarized. As it can be seen, with respect to MVA and TVA, OP 349, OP 368 and OP 374 are the three 
paths that have the highest negative values, in that order, with those of OP 349 being significantly more 
negative. With respect to EWC, OP 167, OP 139 and OP 153 are the three highest values.

Due to the extent of critical paths, only those paths associated with high potential MVA’s and TVA’s have 
been illustrated in Figure 5-6. Those associated with high potential EWC values are not depicted in this 
figure, but for the case of the EWC values, it is not the actual path that is of importance, but the units that 
they contain. Therefore instead of identifying the units by illustrating the paths, as was done previously, the 
units included in the paths associated with the critical EWC values are instead summarized in Table 5-9.   
From Table 5-8 it can be seen, that in the case of water, SustainPro has identified 5 paths with high 
potential TVA’s. Hence SustainPro has recognized that a lot of raw material water from multiple inputs is 
being wasted. From Figure 5-6  it can be seen, that the critical loss of water in all cases happens through 
S75. It is also apparent that the highly negative TVA value for OP 276 is due to the loss of the CLS added 
through S72 with waste water stream S75. However it should be remembered that in the model, CLS is an 
inert, and hence the entire amount of CLS added to the process is not utilized and exits the system with the 
waste water hence losing a lot of value.  
==PAGE:124==
116 | P a g e  
 
 
 
The highly negative MVA value for OP 349 is represented by the light green path in Figure 5-6, and as it can 
be seen is due to the large loss of feedstock cellulose with solid waste stream S63. As it can be seen, paths 
OP 354 and OP 374 are also represented by the light green path and hence larger quantities of 
hemicellulose and lignin from the feedstock is also lost with S63. Besides these paths, it can be seen that 
cellulase in OP 368, which is associated with the second highest negative MVA value, also is lost through 
S63. The reason why this path has attained the second highest negative MVA, despite its low flow rate, is 
due to the high unit price of cellulase which has been assigned an input price of 1.5 USD/kg 
The loss of the small amount of ethylene glycol through S81, as represented by the dark green path (OP 
344), has also been assigned with a negative MVA for the same reasons as cellulose, with a purchase unit 
price at 1.19 USD/kg. C23 is a cycle path also associated to ethylene glycol. The highpotential EWC and AF 
values found for this path might be improved by using a better solvent. However as it was mentioned 
previously, Morales (43; 2) already reviewed many solvent candidates in connection with establishing the 
base case, and this aspect is therefore assumed to already have been performed, leading to ethylene glycol 
being one of the best options. Another option proposed by Morales which also yielded a good performance 
was the ionic liquid [EMIM]+[BF4], for which a similar flowsheet also was established (43). Therefore this 
aspect is assumed covered, and the identified values found associated with the EG paths are therefore not 
addressed further.  
Lastly, it can also be seen that the orange path, representing the production of ammonium sulfate and 
ammonium acetate in R2 which subsequently are removed from the process with waste stream S27 have 
also been assigned highly negative MVA values. This is explained by the fact that these two compounds are 
directly connected to the input of ammonia through S21, which has a purchase price of 0.745 USD/kg.  
When looking at Table 5-9, it can be see that for the 24 paths that have been identified with high EWC 
values, all units but P1, E2, R7 and c-T3 are included. Based on this, all these unit operations but the latter 
mentioned, are considered hotspots identified by PRO II with respect to energy cost. In addition to this, 
based on the material loss bottlenecks identified, is apparent that a lot of raw material is lost through 
mainly waste streams S75 and S63, and that focus therefore should be on revision of these waste streams.

5.2.3 Step 1.3 – Full economic evaluation 
As usual, first the equipment sizing parameters are calculated as demonstrated earlier. Regarding the 
column condensers c-T1 and c-T2, the area calculations are based on a U value of 0.567 kW/m2·°C as 
assumed for E5 and E6 as well. This assumes that ethanol, which is the main component in the hot process 
stream passing through c-T1, also can be assumed to be an organic solvent, which is the case for c-T2, 
where the main component in the hot process stream is ethylene glycol. For c-T3 a value of 1.1357 
kW/m2·°C since the hot process stream here also mainly consists of water. For all three condensers cooling 
water is used, and hence the cold fluid temperatures used to determine the areas are t1 = 20°C and t2 = 
30°C. For r-T1 an overall heat transfer coefficient of U = 1.4196 kW/m2·°C, is assumed since the cold process 
stream mainly is water, and the utility steam. In the case of r-T2 and r-T3, U = 150 Btu/ft2·hr·º (18) = 0.852 
kW/m2·°C was assumed, as this is a typical value for the cold stream being an organic solvent and the hot 
fluid being steam. Reboiler r-T1 utilizes LP steam (T1=T2 = 164 °C), while r-T2 requires MP steam (T1=T2 = 
184 °C). Reboiler r-T3 is assumed to use HP steam and thus the area is based on hot fluid temperatures of 
T1=T2= 254°C.  
 The capacities for the pumps, mixers and splitters, with the exception of M12 (Slurry tank), were as 
previously determined, based on the processed volumetric rates, obtained from PRO II. The flash units are 
cost-estimated as vessels. 
With respect to the filtration units, the pre-treatment reactor, ion exchange, overliming tank, 
reacidification tank, slurry tank, seed holding tank and the seed and production fermenters, the prices 
estimated in the original NREL publication by Wooley et al. (44) for the corresponding units are assumed to 
be valid, as the flowsheet is similar and the input flow rates equivalent to those of this current base case.  
Here the pretreatment reactor system was estimated to have a capital cost of 12461841 USD. The 
solid/liquid filter press modeled by SC1 was estimated to cost 200000 USD a piece, and a total of 8 were 
required for the task yielding a total cost of 1600000 USD. The filter modeled by SC2 is assumed to cost 
200000 USD. The ion exchange unit was estimated to cost 2058000 USD, and the overliming tank 71000 
USD. The reacidification tank and the slurry tank were estimated to 147800 USD and 44800 USD 
respectively, while the rotary drum filter, was estimated to cost 165000 USD.  
The 5 seed fermenters at 20, 194, 1950, 19444 and 194500 gallons respectively, in each of the two trains, 
were estimated to cost 14700 USD, 32600 USD, 81100 USD, 39500 USD and 147245 USD respectively. 
Hence a purchase price of 630290 USD is assumed for R5. The SCCF seed tank was estimated to cost 
161593 USD, while the filter modeled by SC4 was assumed to cost 200000 USD.  
==PAGE:128==
120 | P a g e  
 
 
 
For the centrifuge modeled by SO1, a purchase cost of 2000000 USD is assumed based on the price of 
approximately 2 million. USD for a system of 3 centrifuges performing and equvilent task. 
With respect to the production SSCF fermenters, for 17 units of 962651 gallons each, a total price of 
8387647 USD was estimated.  
All the sizing parameters capital costs price estimated are found on the CD-ROM, due to the extent of the 
table. Here it can also be seen, that a most of the equipment is cost estimated based on the material being 
stainless steel. The need for stainless steel is due to the presence of sulfuric acid in the process, which over 
time would corrode normal carbon steel. Only a few pieces of equipment can settle for carbon steel, for 
example E5 and E6, as no sulfuric acid is present in the process streams at this point in the process. The 
same holds for M1, M7, M13, M16 and M22, as well as the components of distillation columns T1 and T2. 
The estimated utility cost obtained from ECON as well as those determined manually through estimating 
the required utility flow rates are seen below in Table 5-10. The utility cost estimates determined by ECON 
have also been depicted in Figure 5-7.

When consulting Figure 5-7 it is immediately apparent, that two are associated with significantly higher utility 
costs than the other heating requiring units. These are E4 and r-T1, which also were the two units with the 
highest heating demands. Based on this it can be concluded that E4 and r-T1 are the process bottlenecks 
with respect to utility cost.   
 
Figure 5-7. Utility costs associated with each unit operation. Blue = cooling utility costs, orange = electrical costs and red = 
heating utility costs.  
 
For the full economic analysis unit prices from Table 5-7 were assumed for raw materials and product. The 
steam prices of 0.012 USD/kg and 0.016 USD/kg for LP steam and HP steam from Table 2-6 are based on, that 
an amount of water continuously circulates in a steam production loop. Therefore for the LP and HP steam 
directly injected to the process, input prices of 0.0123 USD/kg and 0.0163 USD/kg respectively are 
assumed, in order to account for the water that needs to be added continuously to the steam loop, since 
the injected steam does not return as condensate. 
Once again a land cost of 2% of the TCI has been assumed, which amounts to 4 million USD. Inflation rates 
of 2% and an income tax rate of 39% are again assumed. This time however, a project life time of 20 years 
is used. The outcome of the economic evaluation is summarized in Table 5-11. From these results it can be 
seen that the process only has a rate of return of 1 %, a payback time of 16.34 years and  results in a total 
net profit of 43.42 million USD over 20 years.

5.2.4 Step 1.4 – Environmental impact analysis 
The resultant carbon footprint results are stated below in Table 5-12 and given visually in Figure 5-8.  
By comparing Figure 5-4 with Figure 5-8 is can be seen that, the extent of each units energy demand again is 
directly proportional to the size of their carbon footprint. Therefore the three units with the highest carbon 
footprints consequently are c-T1, r-T1 and E4. However, the carbon footprint of c-T1 is lower than that of 
E4, despite its energy demand being higher, and this is again reasoned by E4’s duty requiring steam and c-
T1’s requiring cooling water. Based on these results, c-T1, E4 and r-T1 are those units to be considered the 
process bottlenecks with respect to carbon footprint.

5.2.5 Step 1.5 – Design Targets 
Table 5-13 summarizes which unit operations were identified as bottlenecks in each analysis. From this table 
it is apparent that E4 and r-T1 have been found to be global bottlenecks.  
In addition to this, it should be remembered that SustainPro also detected critical raw material losses of 
water, cellulose, hemicellulose, lignin and cellulase through waste streams S75 and S63. Here especially the 
loss of cellulose through OP 349 with S63 yielded a significantly high negative MVA value, and should be of 
high focus as the issue which can save the most money if improved. Furthermore it should be remembered 
that the water steam injected to the process with streams S2, S8 and S38 have been taken into account at a 
much lower input value than is the case, and hence SustainPro has not recognized the large material value 
loss that occurs for the water input through these steams. From Table 5-11, it can be seen that the costs 
connected to the input of the LP and HP steam amounts to approximately 8 million USD annually, which is 
approximately 10 times the total cost connected to the input of fresh water. The above issue should 
therefore also be addressed in addition to the energy demands associated to the identified global 
bottlenecks.  
Based on the latter, the main issues identified throughout the full sustainability analysis can be formulated 
as given below: 
 
Raw material (water) loss is high  
 
Feedstock raw material constituents (cellulose, hemicellulose, lignin, cellulase) loss is high 
 
Utility cost is very high in heat exchanger E4  
 
Utility cost is very high in reboiler r-T1  
 
Carbon footprint is high for heat exchanger E4 
 
Carbon footprint is also high for reboiler r-T1 
Accordingly design targets in order to overcome these issues are: 
 
Decrease water loss significantly  
 
Decrease feedstock raw material constituents loss significantly 
 
Decrease utility cost associated with E4 
 
Decrease utility cost associated with r-T1 
 
Decrease carbon footprint of E4 
 
Decrease carbon footprint of r-T1 
 
==PAGE:133==
125 | P a g e  
 
 
 
Table 5-13. Summary of main identified bottlenecks by SustainPro, LCSoft and ECON.

The possible measures that can be taken to meet these design targets are now proposed. For the aspect of 
decreasing the water loss, it was seen that the water loss occurs mainly through S75, and hence recycling of 
the water in this stream back to the raw material water input should be attempted. The water purity of S75 
is 99.01%, and hence the stream contains only minor impurities. A complete recycle of the stream will 
result in the CLS in S75 to highly accumulate in the process, since it in the model functions as an inert. 
Therefore a need for pretreatment of S75 in order to remove most of the CLS and the other impurities is 
required. Here the addition of a membrane for treatment of the water is a good option.  
With respect to the loss of the feedstock constituents with solid waste stream S63, one possibility is to 
recycle the cellulose, hemicellulose and cellulase. The lignin is an inert part of the feedstock, which does 
not take place in any reactions and hence should be removed before recycling to avoid accumulation in the 
process. This separation might be complicated. However another option is to utilize the solid waste stream 
by combusting it in the on-site combustor, responsible for delivering heat to the steam production loop. 
Therefore fuel credit can be assumed for this stream, assuming that an boiler already exists, allowing 
savings to be achieved in terms of utility preparation costs.     
==PAGE:134==
126 | P a g e  
 
 
 
The utility cost of E4 can be reduced by means of heat integration. For example, the temperature of S75 is 
approximately 100 °C and will require cooling, before processing in a membrane. This stream could 
therefore be sent through E4 or possibly a newly added heat exchanger prior to E4 for preheating, if the 
entire heating demand in E4 can’t be covered by S75. In this way the heating demand of E4 can be reduced 
by simultaneously obtaining a cooling of S75. An extra option is to utilize the heat in the vapor stream from 
flash F1, which has a temperature of approximately 100 °C. In addition to this S11 is 99.08% pure in water, 
and hence by passing this stream through the membrane after being utilized as heat transfer medium, an 
increased recovery of waste water can be achieved.  
With respect to reducing the energy demand and hence the utility cost associated with r-T1, it can once 
again be assessed whether an increase in capital cost in the form of an increased number of trays can bring 
down the heating requirement of r-T1. The aspect of heat integration might also by be explored, if the later 
won’t allow a reduction. However based on the temperatures of the streams that are cooled throughout 
the process, compared to the high temperature of the hot stream output from r-T1, only the hot process 
streams cooled in E5 and E6 (S80 and S83 respectively) have temperatures high enough for heat integration 
with r-T1. However in comparing the flow rates, S75 has a flow rate of almost 10 times larger than those of 
S80 and S83, and hence heat integration involving r-T1 is not likely.  
By decreasing the energy demand and consequently utility costs of E4 and r-T1, the last two design targets 
requirements will also automatically get satisfied, as a reduced energy demand is expected to yield reduced 
carbon footprints.  
5.3 Part 2 – Generation and screening of new design alternatives 
5.3.1 Step 2.1 – Generation of new design alternatives 
5.3.1.1 New design alternative 1 
In this design alternative, the first design target with respect to the water loss through S75 is addressed. 
S75 has a total flow rate of 319457.4 kg/hr, out of which 307553.1 kg/hr is water and hence S73 holds a lot 
of water for recycle. As mentioned, a cooling of S75 is necessary, and this can be done by transferring the 
heat present in S75 to S65 which is to be heated to 92.85 °C. Initially, passing S75 through E4 only allowed 
partial heating, if the heat exchanger area was to be kept within appropriate limits. Therefore an extra heat 
exchanger (E7) is added prior to E4, which utilizes S75 as heating medium in order to preheat S65.The 
remaining heating duty is then accomplished in E4. This results in E7 being able to preheat S65 to 69°C and 
at the same time cool S75 to approximately 58 °C. Before recycling the water, treatment of the water using 
a membrane has been chosen.  
==PAGE:135==
127 | P a g e  
 
 
 
Investigation led to the assumption of this membrane being a reverse osmosis (RO) membrane unit, which 
is a widely accepted unit operation for industrial water purification, being especially efficient in 
concentrating sugars and organic acids from water (46; 47; 48). Typical RO water recoveries obtainable are 
between 70-90% (47; 46), while sugar and organic acid retentions exceeding 95% have been reported (46). It 
was found that typical upstream pressures are between 150 and 600 psig (47), and an upstream pressure of 
25.66 atm is therefore assumed, with a permeate pressure of 1 atm. Therefore a pump (P9) is added in 
order to increase the pressure of the cooled waste water to this pressure.  
It is assumed, that the temperature of the cooled waste water exiting the pump at 59 °C is a suitable 
membrane operating temperature and no further conditioning is required before feeding to the membrane 
unit. A stream calculator (SC6) is added in order to model the membrane, and a water recovery of 70% is 
specified, while for the impurities, a retention of 95% has been specified, allowing only 5% of the impurities 
to exit with the water permeate. The unrecovered water and the retained sulfuric acid, CLS etc. is taken as 
a new waste water stream for disposal.  
The almost completely pure water (99.999%) permeate leaves the membrane at a flow rate of 215292.4 
kg/hr. It is led to a newly added splitter (SP2) which splits the stream into three, which are fed to mixers 
M2, M5 and M7. The splitter is modeled in order to supply M2 with 47092 kg water/hr, M5 with 128033 kg 
water/hr and M7 with the residual amount. In this manner, the water input required from S5 and S15 can 
be completely depleted., while the input through S22 can be reduced 
Three controllers are added in order to adjust the flow rate inputs of S5, S15 and S22 according to the 
amount received from the recycle, so the total water input coming out from M2, M5 and M7 are equivalent 
to the base case amounts.  
Another aspect that is taken into account is the design target requiring a reduction in loss of cellulose, 
hemicellulose, lignin and cellulase with solid waste stream S63. Here the solution is to combust the waste 
stream in a combustor. This does not require any physical alterations to the flowsheet, as the utility 
production area including an boiler has been omitted. Hence in theory, S63 is altered from an actual solid 
waste stream to a fuel credit stream, that is sent to the omitted on-site boiler, and in this manner yields 
utility production cost savings.  
The physical alterations included in the new design alternative flow sheet are seen in Figure 5-9. The 
remaining parts of the process flowsheet are identical to those of the base case design.  
==PAGE:136==
128 | P a g e  
 
 
 
5.3.1.2 New design alternative 2 
This second design alternative further considers the possibility of recovering more water and reducing the 
raw material water input further. Two other waste streams containing noteworthy amounts of water exist. 
These are the vapor stream S11 and mixed stream S27, which are 99.08% and 99.26% pure in water 
respectively. The reason why SustainPro has not recognized the loss of water through these waste streams 
is due to the amounts of water in these two only amounting to 43724.43 kg/hr and 87870.41 kg/hr, which 
are small compared to the water flow rate that was lost with S75. 
In addition to its water content, S11 also contains heat that can be utilized to further reduce the heating 
duty of E4. Stream S27 is only approximately 40°C but besides water only contains solid ammonium sulfate 
and ammonium acetate. Therefore by removing the insoluble solids in an centrifuge, pure water can be 
obtained from this stream for direct recycling, without the need for treatment in the membrane.  
A mixer (M19) is added and S75 is mixed with S11 before passing directly through E4. The increased flow 
rate due to the recovery of S11 now allows the mixed waste water stream, which now also contains water 
vapor, to completely satisfy the entire heating duty required to heat S65 to 92.85°C. Hence E7 can 
therefore be removed again. However the increased flow rate and presence of vapor only lead to a slight 
cooling of the combined waste water stream, and therefore additional cooling before processing the waste 
water in the membrane is required. A new E7 is again added to the flowsheet, this time with the purpose of 
cooling the waste water stream further to the previously obtained value of 58.41°C, before increasing the 
pressure of the stream in P9 and feeding to the membrane.   
As mentioned, S27 also contained water for recovery, and is therefore passed through a centrifuge 
modeled by a new solid separator SO2, which is assumed to completely remove all the solids present. A 
new solids waste stream (S98) emerges, while recovering all the water in a completely pure water stream 
exiting the centrifuge. This water stream is bypassed the membrane, and a mixer (M20) is added in order to 
mix this with the membrane-treated waste water. However this yields a recycled water stream which is far 
larger than the water input through S5, S15 and S22 and hence more water has been recovered, than is 
needed fed to the process. A fraction of the combined S75 and S11 waste water is therefore removed via a 
new splitter (SP3), placed prior to the conditioning in E7 and P1, in order to reduce the load on these units 
as well as the membrane, instead of disposing of the extra water after using unnecessary resources to treat 
this water. The fraction removed via SP3 is specified in such a way that the total mixed stream of treated 
water led to SP2 is sufficient enough to allow the remaining fresh water input through S15 to be completely 
depleted as well. This lets the recycled water completely supply the processes total fresh water 
==PAGE:137==
129 | P a g e  
 
 
 
requirement, with water only being continuously fed to the process through the moisture of the feedstock 
and the water in the cellulase input streams.  
The alterations included in this second design alternative flow sheet are seen in Figure 5-10.  
5.3.1.3 New design alternative 3 
In this design alternative, the aspect of utilizing the extra water removed from SP3 for production of steam 
to be recycled back in order to reduce the direct inputs of steam to the process and hence possibly improve 
the overall profitability of the process. In theory this water could be treated in the membrane in order 
improve the purity of the water allowing it to be recycled to the already assumed existing steam production 
area (receiving solid waste stream S63 for combustion in an boiler) and taking a 0.0003 USD/kg as savings 
on this account. However in order to get an idea of how much it would cost to turn the extra waste water 
into steam, and compare it to the raw material steam cost, which we saw in Figure 5-7 was approximately 8 
million USD/year, a separate steam producing section for this purpose is included to the process flowsheet 
in this alternative. 
The splitter SP3 is removed, and the entire combined S75 and S27 waste water stream is treated in the 
membrane, while the splitter SP3 is added after the centrifuge SO2 in order to split the exit stream into 
two, one to bypass everything and be mixed with the membrane permeate via M20, and one for 
production of both LP and HP steam. This stream contains sufficient water to produce the amount of LP 
steam added by S2 and S38, as well as for production of the amount of HP steam added to the process via 
S8. A Pump (P10) is added in order to increase the pressure of the stream meant for steam production to 
13 atm. The next step is to heat this stream to the same temperature as the delivered HP steam via S8, 
which is at 268 °C. This will require heating with either natural gas in a fired heater.  
In order to reduce the energy demand to be supplied via natural gas, the heating is to be accomplished in 
two subsequent heat exchangers. For the first heat exchanger, E7 is moved down to accomplish preheating 
of this stream, integrating cooling of the combined waste water stream. Even after integrated cooling 
through E4 and E7, further cooling is still required and an additional extra heat exchanger E8 is therefore 
inserted to complete the cooling of this stream to 58.41°C. This allows the hot stream outlet temperature 
from E7 to have a temperature of 98°C, and the remaining heating to 268°C is completed in (E9), which in 
theory is a fired heater which must be supplied with natural gas. After this heat exchanger, HP steam at the 
correct conditions have been acquired and a splitter (SP4) is added in order to split the steam into a 
fraction ready for input to M3. The remaining HP steam is sent through an expander in order to reduce the 
pressure and consequently the temperature to 13.2 atm and 164°C, matching the conditions of the LP 
==PAGE:138==
130 | P a g e  
 
 
 
steam injected through S2 and S38. A final splitter (SP5) is added to split the produced LP steam into two 
streams for input to mixers M1 and M10. 
Again three additional controllers adjusting the feed flow rate of S2, S8 and S38 according to the steam 
flow rate supply from the recycled water are added. The addition of the steam producing section has led to 
a slight reduction in the recycle water to splitter SP2. Hence the recycled water supply through S94 no 
longer is large enough for complete eradication of the supply through S22, and hence a small amount is 
required to be supplied continuously through S22. Streams S5 and S15 are continuously depleted.    
The alterations included in the new design alternative flow sheet are seen in Figure 5-11.  
5.3.1.4 New design alternative 4 
In this last design alternative, the design target regarding a reduction of the heating demand associated 
with reboiler r-T1 is assessed. Initially a shortcut column is utilized in order to investigate whether 
increasing the number of theoretical steps can lead to reduced column duties. However here it was found, 
that by increasing by many theoretical steps only gave minor duty reductions. It was then investigated 
whether a slight increase of the input stream temperature would allow a slight decrease in the reboiler 
duty without incurring any extra cost. It was found that the combined waste water stream a slight further 
heating of S86 in E4 to 94 °C. In addition to this an optimizer was added with the purpose of determining 
the optimal feed tray position, which allowed the lowest reboiler duty. Here it was found, that an optimal 
feed tray was at stage 11. The altered feed tray and slightly increased temperature of the feed stream gives 
a slight reduction in the duty of r-T1.  
Due to the only small reduction obtained in r-T1’s duty, it is attempted save some extra money elsewhere 
in the process. It became apparent that the cooling of the effluent streams from T2 and T3 in E5 and E6 
respectively, could be integrated with additional preheating of the water faction for steam production. By 
adding two extra heat exchangers (E10 and E11) between E7 and E9 and integrating the cooling of S80 and 
S83 with further preheating of S104 in the two new heat exchangers, the cooling utilities of E5 and E6 can 
be reduced as well as more importantly, the heating duty associated with E9. The integration allows 
preheating of S104 further to 119 °C in E10 and even further to 145°C in E11. Subsequently S80 and S83 are 
returned to E5 and E6 respectively at lower temperatures which will yield reduced cooling demands and 
hence utility costs connected to heat exchanger E5 and E6.  
The alterations included in the last new design alternative flow sheet is seen in Figure 5-12. Note that the 
altered feed stage is not shown visually but incorporated to the model results through the optimizer unit.

5.3.2 Step 2.2 – New design alternatives rigorous simulation 
The obtained simulation mass and energy balance results can be found on the attached CD-ROM at the 
back. The calculated duties and PRO II estimated sizing parameters for each design alternative are given 
below in Table 5-14. The electricity using units are not included in the last utility row.  In Table 5-15, the overall 
energy demands are summarized.  
Once again, in order to justify the profitability of the alterations done, a few simple economic calculations 
are performed based on the simulation results. Looking at ND 1 quick cost estimations led to an additional 
capital investment of 6263311 USD for the membrane, P9, E7 and SP2. The majority of this extra capital 
investment is due to the membrane, which has been assumed to have a capital cost of 6 million USD. This 
assumption was made based on scaling a capital cost connected to another situation in which RO was used 
for waste water treatment (49). This capital cost may in reality be significantly smaller. The reduction of E4’s 
duty from 87.72 GJ/hr to 33.02 GJ/hr results in an approximate savings of 3577962 USD/year, while an 
additional savings of 542915 USD/year with respect the water feed reduction. Taking the added duty of P9 
estimated to be around 112264 USD/year, and overall savings of approximately 4 million USD/year has 
been estimated. This annual savings allows the extra capital investment to be paid of relatively fast, and 
hence over all the alterations are rendered to be lucrative. In addition to this, an extra income can be taken 
into account for fuel credit from S63. This aspect is addressed when performing the full economic 
evaluations.  
Addressing the second design alternative, quick cost estimates yielded that the addition of new units 
summed up to an additional capital investment of approximately 612000 USD. The complete integration of 
E4’s heating demand with this alternative, yields and additional savings of 2158810 USD/year. In addition to 
this, an additional amount of 139162 USD/year is saved due to the increased amount of recycled water. 
Taking the added utility cost of E7, which in this design alternative no longer is an integrated heat 
exchanger and has been associated with a cooling duty of 55.23 GJ/hr, into account, as well as the savings 
obtained due to the reduced duty of P9, an overall annual saving of 2152339 USD/year compared to ND1 
has been achieved. This also allows the increased capital cost to be paid off within approximately 3.5 years 
and hence the changes made to this alteration is also concluded as overall profitable.

With respect to ND 3, quick capital cost estimates showed that the newly added equipment would amount 
to a total extra capital investment of roughly 1.2 million USD. From Table 5-14 it can be seen that the duty of 
P9 has increased again, due to the treatment of the fraction previously removed by SP3. However this only 
constitutes extra utility costs of around 33000 USD/year. However the newly added E8 will have an 
estimated utility cost of 173231 USD/year, while E9’s utility cost will amount to roughly 7.6 million 
USD/year, based on a natural gas cost of 6 USD/kg. The added expander EX1 has a duty of 85.98 kW which 
will result in a utility cost of 43712 USD/year. However the energy demand of E7 in ND2 has been 
eliminated, and taking this into account, and overall annual utility cost increase of approximately 7752286 
USD/year results. However simultaneously an savings of around 8.1 million USD/years due to the 
elimination of the steam input through S2, S8 and S38 has been obtained, overall resulting in a savings 
estimate of 369342 USD/year, an amount which would require a payback time of around 4 years in order to 
pay back the extra capital investment. Hence the alterations made in this design alternative also seem to be 
beneficial. However the overall savings is not that large, and the increased capital investment will lead to 
increased  operating labor, maintenance and repair costs. If the increase in the latter is larger than the 
overall savings obtained, the operational cost might have increased despite the savings obtained, and the 
alterations might not turn out to be profitable.  
Finally regarding ND 4, the addition of E10 and E11 is estimated to only bring an extra capital investment of 
on 60430 USD. However they will result in a reduction of the heating demand of E9 to 139.16 GJ/hr 
corresponding to a savings of 538404 USD/year. From Table 5-14 it can also be seen that the cooling duties of 
E5 and E6 are reduced to 0.81 and 6.75 GJ/hr respectively, and a slight reduction to 100.05 GJ/hr in r-T1 has 
also be obtained, due to the increased input stream temperature. Taking all of these into account, an 
overall utility savings estimate of 692502 USD/year is achieved, which will allow rapid payback of the extra 
capital investment. The alterations made to this final new design alternative are therefore also found to be 
highly profitable.

with a heat of evaporation for water of 2264.74 kJ/kg, a steam price of 0.012 USD/kg and a boiler efficiency 
of 80%. Based on this SustainPro determines fuel credits for the 5 mentioned components in S63, taking 
these into account when determining the MVA indicators for the open paths ending in S63.

Table 5-18 below summarizes the main economic metrics acquired from the full economic analyzes for 
each new design alternative, It should be noted, that here the aspect of utilizing S63 for combustion in an 
on-site boiler in order to save on the steam production costs has also been taken into account. Based on 
the SustainPro determined fuel credit prices, an average fuel credit value of 0.099 USD/kg can be assigned 
to stream S63. Multiplying this price with the flow rate of S63, a total fuel credit of approximately 29 million 
USD/year is estimated to be able to be obtained by fully combusting the entire solid waste stream. This 
highly exceeds the utility costs associated with heating, which from Table 5-17 highest in ND 3 amounting 
to around 19.8 million USD/year. Hence full combustion of S63 can lead to production of more steam than 
==PAGE:148==
140 | P a g e  
 
 
 
actually is required by the process. It is therefore assumed that this excess steam is converted to electricity 
for use in the plant or for sale to the grid.

Comparing with the economic metrics for the base case from Table 5-11, it can be seen that the total 
purchase cost has increased by 6271402 USD, mainly due to the addition of the membrane, resulting in an 
TCI increase of around 35 million USD in going from the base case to ND 1. The annual energy consumption 
has decreased, mainly due to the heating duty reduction of E4 obtained by adding and integrating the 
preheating heat exchanger E7. This is also seen in the utility cost reduction, which has been reduced by 
approximately 4.3 million USD/year. However, despite the utility cost reduction and the obtained savings 
on water input due to the recycled water, which yielded a savings of around 500000 USD/year, an increase 
of approximately 6 million USD/year in the annual operating cost has resulted compared to the base case. 
This can be explained by the high increase of the TCI, and hence the fixed capital investment, on which the 
estimated operation labor, supervision, maintenance and repair costs are based on, as these are 
determined as fractions of the FCI. Therefore the latter costs have increased significantly, resulting in an 
overall increase in the annual operating costs. However despite the increase in operating cost, a significant 
increase in net profit is obtained. This is due to extra income due to the fuel credit of S63, which in the base 
case was just a waste stream for disposal. Consequently the rate of return and payback time has also 
improved. Four all design alternatives significantly higher net profits have been achieved, mainly due to the 
utilization of the solid waste stream. However, as it can be seen ND 2 is found to be the alternative that 
achieves the highest estimated net profit of 400.75 million USD over a project life time of 20 years.  
In going from ND 1 to ND 2, it can be seen that the TCI has increased further by around 3.5 million USD, due 
to addition of SO2 and two mixers and a splitter. The annual energy consumption has increased despite the 
fact that the remaining 33.1 GJ/hr heating required by E4 has been integrated. However E7 is now no 
longer integrated and has been associated with a cooling duty of 55.23 GJ/hr. In addition to this, the 
cooling duty of R2 has also increased slightly. Despite the increase in energy demand, a shift from heating 
energy to cooling energy has occurred, and explains the utility cost reduction of around 1.9 million 
==PAGE:149==
141 | P a g e  
 
 
 
USD/year. This together with savings of approximately 140000 USD/year on water input has despite the 
increased TCI and hence operating labor etc., still allowed a decrease in the overall operating cost to 
158885712 USD/year. This decreased annual operating cost consequently also leads to an improved overall 
profitability of the process.  
Comparing ND 2 to ND 3, it can be seen that again an increase in the TCI has resulted, due to the addition 
of the newly added equipment. The increased purchase cost of approximately 1.2 million USD has led to an 
increase of the TCI by 6.6 million USD. The energy consumption has increased significantly, mainly due to 
the addition of E9, which is connected to a heating demand of 139 GJ/hr. This is also seen as an increase in 
the utility cost of 7.7 million USD/year. This is however counteracted by the obtained savings of around 8.1 
million USD/year due to the steam production from the recycled water. However despite this,  the 
increased TCI leads to an increase in the cost associated with labor, maintenance, etc. that is larger than 
the savings obtained. Hence the annual operating cost ends up increasing, setting back the net profit, 
payback time and rate of return.   
Proceeding to ND 4, it can be seen that only a slight increase in the TCI happens, compared to ND 3. This 
small increase is due to the addition of only E10 and E11, which only incurs an extra capital investment of 
around 60430 USD. However the addition of these induces an energy reduction in E5, E6 and E9. In addition 
to this a small increase in the duty of r-T1 also is achieved in this alternative, and consequently an overall 
reduced energy consumption and utility cost is achieved. The improvement of the latter results in a 
reduced annual operating cost and consequently also an improvement of the overall profitability of the 
process, in going from ND 3 to ND 4.  
The obtained carbon footprint results for each the new design alternatives are summarized in the table 
below, Table 5-19. Here it should be noted, that due to the combustion of the solid waste now utilized in 
the preparation of steam, all the heating demands are associated with a utility type given as ‘Wood waste, 
combusted in industrial boiler’, considering the contents of S63 as wood waste.

5.4 Part  3 – Comparison of base case design against new design alternatives 
5.4.1 Step 3.1 – Comparison of  full sustainability results 
The duty requirements of the base case are illustrated together with those of the new design alternatives 
as seen in Figure 5-14.  
Looking at the columns representing R2, it can be seen that in the base case R2 required heating duty. 
However in the first design alternative this duty has changed to a cooling duty. This can be explained by an 
increase in the inlet temperature to R2 from approximately 33 to 39 °C. This is a result of the recycle water, 
which is not cooled before feeding to the process and hence the water input is now water at 59°C instead 
of water at 20°C. In the remaining alterations, this temperature is slightly altered due to the changes made 
to the recyle water setup, explaining the variations of R2’s cooling duty. This also hols for the variations in 
==PAGE:151==
143 | P a g e  
 
 
 
R1’s duty. When looking at R3, R4, R5 and R6+7 it can be seen that their duties have not been affected 
throughout all the alterations.  
Considering the heat exchangers it can be seen that the cooling duties of E1 and E3 also vary, once again 
this is due to the variations of the recycled water temperature, which directly affects the temperatures of 
the input streams to these two heat exchangers. However it does not have any effect on E2, which 
maintains a constant cooling duty throughout all design alternatives. For E4 the heating duty reduction due 
to the addition of the integrated E7 leads to an approximately 55 GJ/hr reduction, which instead emerges 
as a heating duty performed by E7. However this heating duty is satisfied by a process stream and does not 
incur any extra costs.  
Continuing to ND 2 it can be seen that the duty of E4 is increased to its initial base case value due to the 
entire heating load now being able to be performed in E4 by heat transfer from from the recycled waste 
water stream, simultaneously cooling the latter. Hence this energy demand is integrated and will not incur 
any utility costs. The slight heating demand that is seen in going from ND 3 to ND 4 is of course due to the 
increased heating task of E4 to 94°C versus the base case of 92.85°C. However this extra heating load is still 
satisfied by the heat content in S75 and hence does not add any additional costs. The duties of heat 
exchangers E5 and E6 only vary in going from ND 3 to ND 4, due to the heat integration of the bottoms 
streams from columns T2 and T3 with E10 and E11 allowing. For E7 it is apparent that in ND 1 it was 
associated with a heating duty, as it was placed prior to E4 in order to preheat S86. In ND 2 it was 
connected to a cooling duty for cooling the waste water stream prior to inlet to the membrane. IN ND3 it is 
converted to an integrated heat exchanger than simultaneously cools the waste water stream and heats 
the fraction of waste water sent to steam production and hence again is associated with heating 
For E8 it can be seen that a cooling demand only emerges in ND 3, due to the heat exchanger only being 
added to the process flowsheet in this alternative. Its slight energy reduction that occurs from ND 3 to ND 4 
is explained by the increased heating of S86 in E4 and consequently removing slightly more heat from S75 
before the second round of cooling in E7, and subsequently the final cooling in E8. For E9 it is apparent, 
that a large heating duty emerges in ND 3, which of course is due to the heating task of heating the water 
to 268°C. This duty is however decreased in ND 4 due to the introduction of E10 and E11 which adds extra 
pre-heating steps. The reduction in the duty of E9 emerges as the duties associated with E10 and E11 in ND 
4. Again these heating duties are integrated, receiving heat from the hot bottoms streams from r-T2 and r-
T3 and therefore won’t incur any additional utility costs.  
==PAGE:152==
144 | P a g e  
 
 
 
The duties of pumps P1 though P8 and P10 as well as expander EX1 do not vary. Only the duty of P9 varies 
according to the changing flow rates that are processed in this pump, which increases in going from ND 1 to 
ND 3. The duties of the flash units are also unaffected by the alterations made. This also holds for the r-T2 
and cT2. With respect to c-T1 and r-T1, a small improvement is achieved only in ND 4 due to the increased 
temperature of the input stream and the optimization of the feed tray position.  
The many alterations, including depletion of feed waters streams, recycling of the waste water streams and 
addition of new waste water streams makes it difficult to be able to find comparable mass open paths 
associated with water. Therefore alternatively, the overall water amount fed to the process for each 
alternative is compared below in Figure 5-13, in order to prove that improvements to the raw material water 
loss have been made. In this figure, the water input rates are shown, including the water fed to the process 
in the form of steam. From this figure it is obvious that the water input has improved considerably in going 
from the base case to ND 3, for which a 90.2 % savings has been achieved. Hence in terms of lower water 
consumption, ND 3 and ND 4 offer the best solutions.

In contrary to the paths associated with water, the paths associated with cellulose, hemicellulose, lignin 
and cellulase are comparable. Base case OP 349, OP 354, OP 368 and OP 374 are the paths of cellulose, 
hemicellulose, cellulase and lignin respectively, from added with the feedstock through S1 till the removal 
of the unused residual of the latter components with solid waste stream S63. The aspect of taking fuel 
credit for S63 in the SustainPro analyzes should yield significantly reduced MVA.  
In Table 5-20, the mass indicator results in the above mentioned paths are compared for all design 
alternatives. From this table it is easily seen that in going from the base case design to ND 1, improvements 
have been made. The MVA’s and TVA’s of base case paths OP 349 and OP 374 has been improved 
significantly going from negative values to desirable positive values. This is of course is due to the value of 
cellulose, hemicellulose and lignin being 0.03 USD/kg in the feedstock, an increasing to fuel credit values of 
0.074, 0.075 and 0.113 USD/kg respectively at their exit. The base case OP 354 has also improved to a 
positive TVA value in the new design alternatives, but the extent of the increase is not that large, due to the 
smaller amount of hemicellulose present in S63. A small improvement is also seen for cellulase in base case 
path OP 368. The improved MVA and TVA value is still negative, since the cellulase is added to the process 
at a value of 1.5 USD/year, and removed from the process with a fuel credit value of 0.101 USD/kg. 
Furthermore it can be seen that in continuing to the other design alternatives, only slight variations are 
obtained, since the input of feedstock and hence the flow rate of S63 does not vary considerably. Overall, 
the results of this table proves that improvements with respect to the design target requiring an 
improvement of the raw material loss that occurred through S63, also have been achieved.
