The obtained utility costs for all four designs are visualized below in Figure 5-15. The utility cost changes 
associated with each unit operation are direct results of the duty alterations, and reasoning for the 
variation of the utility cost are equivalent to those given previously for the duty variations. Overall it can be 
seen that the design target requiring utility costs reductions of E4 has been met in each new design 
alternative, while the design target requiring a lower r-T1 utility cost only is met to a minor extent in ND 4. 
==PAGE:155==
147 | P a g e  
 
 
 
The economic metric results obtained from the full economic evaluations have already been discussed 
previously, and here it was seen that the highest obtainable net profit was associated with ND 2.  
The comparison of the environmental impact analysis results have also been depicted visually in Figure 5-16. 
The pumps, which are barely present due to their low electrical works, have been omitted. From this figure 
it can be seen that the variations in each unit’s carbon footprint this time does not corresponds to their 
duty variations for the case of the heating units. It can be seen, that when comparing with the base case 
columns, increased carbon footprints have resulted. Even for those with are associated with a constant 
heating duty through all the alterations.  
This is explained by the change in the preparation method for steam, where the boiler combusts the solid 
waste, which contains more carbon than natural gas, and hence leads to higher CO2 productions associated 
with the steam preparation, as reflected in the carbon footprints requiring steam. The extent of the 
increase is so significant, that the overall carbon footprint has increased by a large amount in all design 
alternatives. Overall the utilization of S63 as fuel to the boiler has an offsetting affect, despite its highly 
economical advantage.  
Despite the above, the complete integration of E4’s heating duty has met the target specification of a lower 
carbon footprint of this global bottleneck. However, for the other global bottleneck r-T1, the opposite 
holds, where the carbon footprint has doubled.

5.4.2 Step 3.2 – Selection of best alternative 
The detailed comparison of a wide range of sustainability metrics are summarized in Table 5-21. The 
deviation given is between the base case design and ND 2.  
The radar plot visually comparing the most important variables from Table 5-21 is seen in Figure 5-17, while the 
percentages this plot is based on is given in Table 5-22. From the radar plot, it is obvious that none of the 
new design alternatives are non-trade off solutions. They all highly exceed the base case with respect to the 
overall carbon footprint and the carbon footprint of bottleneck r-T1. The design that has the lowest overall 
carbon footprint is ND 1.  
With respect to the other metrics, ND 3 and ND 4 also fall outside the boundaries of the base case design 
with respect to energy usage, utility costs, and operational costs, while ND 1 and 2 only fall slightly outside 
with  respect to operating costs. operational costs, which only exceeds that of the base case slightly.   
With respect to energy usage, ND 1 yields the lowest demand, while ND 2 has obtained the lowest overall 
utility costs. However with respect to the design target requiring reduced utility costs of E4, ND 2, ND 3 and 
ND 4 all have completely eliminated this critical utility cost, while ND 1 only offer a considerable reduction. 
However with respect to lower utility cost of the reboiler r-T1, only ND 4 meets this target to a minor 
extent.  
In terms of water recycle, the best solution is given by ND 3 and ND 4, while ND 2 has achieved the best net 
profit.  
Based on the results, it is hard to estimate which of the alternatives are best, and whether they overall are 
that much better than the base case. Only taking the economical aspect into account, they all are more 
profitable, and this aspect mosty is the turning point.  
Overall, based on the radar plot ND 2 is taken to be the best alternative. ND 2 also results in the highest net 
profit, this is however at the expense of of the carbon footprint. One way of looking at it, is that this extra 
capital can be used towards measures of increased treatment of the gas emissions.  
Alternatively, the aspect of combusting S63 in the boiler can be eliminated again, which would still allow 
improvements to have been achieved with respect to raw material loss and a elimination of the duty of E4 
as well as lower energy demands and utility costs, rendering ND 2 to be an overall more sustainable design 
alternative, in which only the design target specifications regarding the reboiler haven’t been met.

6 Conclusion 
Three different processes were assessed with respect to their sustainability potential by means of a 
multilevel computer-aided framework for systematic design of more sustainable chemical processes, with 
the objective of proposing more sustainable process flowsheets, and at the same time testing and verifying 
the performance of the framework.  
In all three cases it was found that a general relationship exists between the energy demand, the utility 
costs and the carbon footprint: High energy demands in unit operations will be accompanied by a 
corresponding high carbon footprint, and if the energy demands are associated, dependent of the type of 
utility used. In the case of cooling demands, if the chosen utility is cooling water, which is often a sufficient 
cooling remedy, the energy demand may be high and consequently the carbon footprint as well, but due to 
the low unit price associated with cooling water, the connected utility costs can be expected to be low 
compared to those associated to heating units. With respect to the carbon footprint a general consistency 
was seen between the extent of the energy demand and its resultant carbon footprint, also being highly 
affected by the nature and production method of the heating utility. In general, heating utilities result in 
larger carbon footprints. Based on the latter, a general conclusion is, that within a process, most often it is 
the unit operations requiring heating that will be associated with the highest utility costs and carbon 
footprints and hence also these that will hold the highest potentials for improvement. Therefore already 
when assessing the demands associated with each piece of equipment, it can be expected that those with 
the highest energy duties will be those identified as process bottlenecks. In addition to this, as it was seen 
in all three cases analyzed, the unit operations with the highest heating demands can also be expected to 
be identified as global bottlenecks, as these both often will be associated with high carbon footprints and 
utility costs. Furthermore it was seen that the aspect of mass integration also can highly improve the 
overall sustainability of a process. 
From the above, a general statement for when improving a process’s overall sustainability can be 
recognized: By reducing the energy demand of the unit operations responsible for the highest demands, an 
overall improvement of the carbon footprints and utility costs associated with these units can be obtained, 
and consequently an improvement in the overall carbon footprint and overall profitability of the process.  
Mass and energy integration were the main measures taken to establish better design alternatives for all 
three base cases. In addition to this, the aspect of increasing the number of theoretical stages in distillation 
columns as means to improve the operating costs was also performed in the conceptual example. Here a 
total of 4 design alternatives were proposed, of which the fourth was found to be the optimal solution 
meeting all the set design targets without compromises with respect to sustainability metrics, offering 
==PAGE:162==
154 | P a g e  
 
 
 
substantial improvements of the overall profitability. For the HDA case study, 3 design alternatives were 
proposed. Here the third design alternative, a non-tradeoff alternative in which all bottlenecks and the 
overall economics of the process are improved significantly. Regarding the bioethanol case study, 4 design 
alternatives were established. Here the second alternative proposed was evaluated to be the alternative 
that best met the design targets. The aspect of utilizing a solid waste stream that was associated with high 
raw material loss, for combustion in a boiler, was associated with high profitability improvements, but 
unfortunately on the expense of the carbon footprint results. The increased profit should therefore 
partially be used to employed better gas emission treatment techniques, or alternatively the aspect of 
combusting the solid waste stream should be eliminated again.  
From the results it is apparent that the step by step implementation of the computer-aided framework is 
generic and led to the proposal of more sustainable design alternatives. Without the framework and its 
incorporated computer tools, the comprehensive assessments would have been difficult and tedious. The 
computer-aided framework has therefore been found to be a very valuable tool for the purpose of 
sustainable process flowsheet design.
